Rulings on Irregular Bleeding: Delaying Ghusl and Prayer
Explains rulings when blood stops and returns, and the case of alternating habit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on delaying ghusl and prayer when menstrual bleeding is irregular, and apply them to cases of intermittent flow.

Core Idea
Delay prayer when blood returns likely, but delay ghusl only as a precaution; intermittent days require separate rules.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the nuanced rules a woman must follow when her bleeding pattern is irregular. The rulings distinguish between recommended precaution and obligatory delay, and you will learn how to handle scenarios where blood stops and returns within the ten-day cycle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key scenarios
The passage covers: (1) blood stops for less than 3 days, (2) blood stops for 10 days or more, (3) alternating habit of one day blood, one day purity for up to 10 days.
Each scenario has different obligations for ghusl and prayer.
2
Determine the ruling for scenario one (stop <3 days)
She must delay the prayer to the end of its time (wajib). If she fears missing it, she performs wudu without ghusl because the blood is istihada. Delaying ghusl is mustahabb (recommended).
The delayed prayer is obligatory due to likelihood of return; ghusl delay is not obligatory.
3
Determine the ruling for scenario two (stop ≥10 days)
After 10 days, she is ruled pure; ghusl becomes obligatory. The maximum menstruation is 10 days; anything beyond is istihada.
This is the standard Hanafi rule: hayd max 10 days.
4
Analyze the alternating habit
A woman who sees blood one day and purity the next must leave prayer and fasting on blood days and perform wudu (not ghusl) on purity days until two consecutive blood days pass. After the third blood day, she performs ghusl and prays on the purity day.
This pattern continues until the tenth day; after that, she may be considered pure.

Source Text

Delaying ghusl by way of recommendation. And if it (blood) stops for less than three days, she delays the prayer to the end of the time; and if she fears missing the prayer, she performs wudu and prays. Then in the mentioned situations, if the blood returns within ten days, the ruling of her purity becomes invalid, whether she is a novice or a habitual woman. So if blood stops for ten days or more, after ten days she is ruled pure, and ghusl is obligatory upon her. And it has been mentioned that the habitual woman whose habit is to see blood one day and purity one day, like that until ten days – when she sees blood she leaves prayer and fasting; when she becomes pure on the second day she performs wudu and prays; then on the third day she leaves prayer and fasting; then on the fourth day she performs ghusl and prays; thus until ten days. [1] His saying: 'by way of recommendation' – meaning it is recommended for her to delay ghusl to the end of the time merely as a precaution and to seek certainty in religion; it is not obligatory upon her. If she performs ghusl and prays without delay, she has not sinned because the return (of blood) is not likely in her case. [2] His saying: 'she delayed' – meaning it is obligatory upon her to delay the prayer to the end of the recommended time because the return is likely. If she fears missing (the prayer) she performs wudu and prays without ghusl because that blood is istihada (non-menstrual). If it returns before or after that within the menstrual period, it is ruled as menstruation, and the intervening purity is not a separator. Precaution in such a case concerning intercourse is to abstain until the matter is certain. [3] His saying: 'then in the mentioned situations' – that is, when it stops for less than three days, and when it stops after that for less than ten days at the beginning of the habit in a habitual woman or before or after it. [4] His saying: 'after ten days' – meaning merely after ten days she is ruled pure; intercourse becomes permissible and ghusl becomes obligatory because menstruation does not exceed ten days; what exceeds it is istihada. [0] His saying: 'and it has been mentioned' – in the passive voice: i.e. in the fatwa collections. [6] His saying: 'she performed wudu and prayed' – because the previous blood, being less than three days, is istihada; so ghusl is not obligatory, only wudu. [7] His saying: 'she performed ghusl and prayed' – due to the passing of the minimum duration of menstruation. [8] His saying: 'thus until ten days' – meaning she leaves prayer and fasting on the day of blood, and performs ghusl and prays (on the day of purity).
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the rulings of menstruation and irregular bleeding. It begins by explaining that delaying ghusl until the end of the prayer time is recommended (mustahabb), not obligatory, simply out of precaution for the possibility that blood might return. However, if a woman's blood stops for less than three days, she must delay the prayer to the end of its time because the return of blood is likely (maznun). If she fears missing the prayer entirely before the end of the time, she performs wudu only (not ghusl) and prays, because that blood is considered istihada (irregular non-menstrual bleeding). If the blood returns within ten days, the earlier ruling of purity is invalidated; the ten-day period is the maximum for menstruation. Then the text discusses a habitual woman whose cycle alternates day by day: one day blood, one day purity. She leaves prayer and fasting on the blood day, and on the purity day she only does wudu and prays (since the purity is less than fifteen days, it is not a complete tuhr). On the fourth day, after two consecutive days of blood, she must perform ghusl and pray, because the minimum period of menstruation (three days) has passed. The pattern continues until ten days. The footnotes clarify the distinctions between obligation, recommendation, and precaution, emphasizing that the rulings are based on likelihood (ghalaba al-zann) and that intercourse is avoided until the situation is certain. The Hanafi position is that the maximum menstrual period is ten days, and anything beyond is istihada.

Key Terms
الاستحباب (al-istihbab): Recommendation; an act that is rewarded if done but not sinful if omitted. Here it refers to delaying ghusl to the end of the prayer time as a precaution.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Irregular vaginal bleeding that is not menstrual; it does not prevent prayer or fasting, but invalidates wudu.
المعتادة (al-mu'tadah): A woman who has an established habit (adah) of menstruation, both in timing and duration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a woman's menstrual blood stops after 5 days and then returns on day 8, which of the following is true?
A) The prayers she performed on days 6-7 are valid because she was pure.
B) The prayers on days 6-7 are invalid because the return of blood within ten days shows the purity was not real.
C) She must perform ghusl again on day 5 to be valid.
D) She should have delayed praying until day 10.

Key Takeaway
Irregular bleeding requires careful distinction between mustahabb and wajib; blood returning within ten days nullifies prior purity.

Minimum and Maximum Duration of Tuhr (Purity)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the maximum duration of tuhr for habitual women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the maximum duration of tuhr for habitual women and explain the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Minimum tuhr is fifteen days; maximum is unlimited except to establish a habit, then six months minus one day.

The period of purity between menstrual cycles (tuhr) has a clear minimum but an unclear maximum. While all Hanafis agree on the fifteen-day minimum, the maximum becomes critical when a woman suffers continuous bleeding and needs to establish a habit for rulings like 'iddah and prayer. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a maximum can be set, exploring the arguments of the majority and a minority who deny any limit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed minimum and general rule
The text states that the minimum tuhr is fifteen days, and the maximum has no limit unless needed to establish a habit.
The minimum is unanimous; the maximum is default unlimited.
2
Recognize the exception for establishing a habit
When a woman suffers continuous bleeding and needs to determine her menstrual habit, scholars set a maximum limit to avoid uncertainty.
This exception applies to habitual women (mu'tadah) and confused women (mutahayyirah).
3
Compare the two Hanafi positions
The majority (including al-'Ayni and the author) hold that the maximum is six months minus one day. Abu 'Ismah and Abu Khazim al-Qadi argue there is no limit at all because no textual evidence exists.
Both positions have their proofs; the majority rely on necessity and transmitted practices.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
An objection is raised: if tuhr less than fifteen days is not valid, then how can the maximum be set at six months? The answer distinguishes between a novice (mubtadi'ah) and a habitual woman.
For a novice, any blood less than fifteen days is menstruation; for a habitual woman, her known habit takes precedence.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical impact
The relied-upon view in the school is the six-month limit, as chosen by the majority. This affects prayer, fasting, and 'iddah calculations.
Al-'Ayni supports this, and it is the position of al-Maydani and Ibn 'Abidin.

Source Text

And the minimum of tuhr (purity) is fifteen days, and there is no limit to its maximum (the minimum of tuhr is fifteen days and there is no limit to its maximum) except for the establishment of a habit. Then the maximum of tuhr is estimated in its regard, then they differed in estimating its duration, and the most correct is that it is estimated as six months minus one day of tuhr. An objection was raised here that this contradicts what was previously discussed, because from it one knows that all (blood) is menstruation in such forms, since the intervening tuhr is not fifteen days. And it was answered that the previous discussion was about the novice (mubtadi'ah), and this is about the habitual woman, and that the first is what the majority chose, and this is a narration from some. [1] His saying: 'and there is no limit to its maximum' – meaning there is no legally prescribed limit beyond which it cannot increase. So she fasts and prays as long as she sees tuhr, even if it occupies her whole life, for it may extend to a year, two years, and more than that. [2] His saying: 'except for the establishment' – meaning the maximum of tuhr has no prescribed limit except for establishing the habit needed in the chapter of 'iddah and others. For the majority, its maximum is prescribed. [3] His saying: 'and the most correct...' – al-'Ayni said in 'al-Binaya Sharh al-Hidaya': 'There was a need to establish a habit when bleeding continues according to the general scholars, contrary to Abu 'Ismah and Abu Khazim al-Qadi, for there is no limit to its maximum absolutely in their view, because the prescription of limits is based on hearing (transmission), and there is no hearing here.' (1) This issue was investigated by al-Birkawi, and Ibn 'Abidin followed him: that if a novice's bleeding continues, her menstrual period each month is ten and her tuhr is twenty, as in most books; rather Nouh Afandi transmitted agreement on it, contrary to what is in 'al-Imdad' that her tuhr is fifteen. The habitual woman is referred to her habit in tuhr as long as it is not six months; then she is referred to six months less one hour, like the confused woman (mutahayyira) called the lost one (dalla), who has forgotten her habit and cannot determine her tuhr and menstruation except in the case of 'iddah in divorce. This is according to the view of al-Maydani which is adopted by the majority; but according to al-Hakim al-Shahid, she is referred to two months. The rest is in 'Radd al-Muhtar' (1:190), and see 'Fath Bab al-'Inaya' (1:205-207). (2) He is Sa'd Mu'ad al-Marwazi... (3) He is 'Abd al-Hamid ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Qadi, Abu Khazim...
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the maximum duration of tuhr (the pure period between menstrual cycles). The main text states that the minimum tuhr is fifteen days by consensus, and there is no maximum limit except for the purpose of establishing a habitual pattern (nasb al-'adah). For a woman who experiences continuous bleeding or has lost her habit, scholars differ on how to estimate the maximum tuhr. The most correct (al-asahh) view according to the commentator is that the maximum is six months minus one day of tuhr. However, an objection is raised that this contradicts an earlier rule that any period of less than fifteen days is not considered a valid tuhr. The answer clarifies that the earlier rule applies to a novice (mubtadi'ah) who has no habit, while this rule applies to a woman with a known habit (mu'tadah). The majority follow the former view, and this latter view is a narration from some scholars. The footnotes elaborate: al-'Ayni in 'al-Binaya' notes that the need to establish a habit arises from continuous bleeding, and he cites a disagreement with Abu 'Ismah and Abu Khazim al-Qadi, who deny any maximum limit at all. Al-Birkawi and Ibn 'Abidin explain that for a novice with continuous bleeding, her tuhr is twenty days per month (since the menstrual maximum is ten). For a habitual woman, her tuhr is based on her habit unless it exceeds six months; if it exceeds, she is considered confused (mutahayyira) and her tuhr is set at six months minus one hour for the purpose of 'iddah. There is also a minority view by al-Hakim al-Shahid that she is referred to two months. The Hanafi school thus has multiple positions, but the relied-upon view is the one described by al-Maydani and adopted by the majority.

Key Terms
الطهر (al-tuhr): The period of purity between two menstrual cycles; also refers to the state of being free from menstrual blood.
نصب العادة (nasb al-'ādah): Establishing a habitual pattern for a woman's menstruation and purity when her bleeding is irregular or continuous, to determine rulings.
المتحيرة (al-mutahayyirah): A woman who is confused about her menstrual habit because she has forgotten the duration or pattern; her case requires special estimation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ten days
B) Fifteen days
C) Twenty days
D) Six months

Key Takeaway
The minimum tuhr is fifteen days; maximum is determined by habit, with the majority setting it at six months minus one day for legal necessities.

Taharah — Purification

Maximum Duration of Purity and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Establishing a Habit
Commentary expanding on the ruling of maximum tuhr with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of maximum tuhr in the Hanafi school and identify the major opinions in the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its estimation when establishing a habit, along with the preferred (mufta bihi) view.

Core Idea
Maximum tuhr is unlimited in principle, but limited to establish a habit; the mufta bihi is 2 months for ease.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on a key exception to the general rule of unlimited maximum purity. When a woman needs to establish a habit for 'iddah or other rulings, the Hanafi school holds that her maximum tuhr must be estimated. This intra-Hanafi dispute reveals how practical necessity ('urf and istihsan) shaped the tarjih process. By the end, you will understand not only the ruling but the legal reasoning behind the choice of the 2-month view.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and the exception
Identify the base ruling: minimum tuhr is 15 days, maximum is unlimited. Then locate the exception: 'except for establishing a habit' (illa li-nasb al-'adah). This is the core tension: unlimited vs limited.
The exception applies when the woman needs a habit for legal purposes like 'iddah.
2
Understand the need for estimation (nasb al-'adah)
When a woman's bleeding continues, scholars need to determine her 'habit' in menstruation to know when she is in tuhr. The maximum tuhr becomes limited in such cases to avoid indefinite delays in rulings like 'iddah.
This is a practical necessity (darurah) that triggers divergent opinions.
3
Present the major opinions
List at least three: (1) Abu 'Isma & Abu Khazim: no limit at all (no transmitted text). (2) Al-Maidani & majority: 6 months minus one hour. (3) Al-Hakim al-Shahid: 2 months (from Muhammad). Also note others: Muhammad ibn Shuja' (19 days), Muhammad ibn Salama (27 days).
Each opinion has its own reasoning; note that al-ashah is the 6-month view, but the fatwa is on 2 months.
4
Explain the tarjih mechanism
Imam Burhan al-Din al-Marghinani chose the 2-month opinion (from al-Hakim al-Shahid) because it is 'easier for the mufti and the women'. This shows that tarjih in later Hanafism often considers practical ease.
The opposing view (no limit) is rejected because it would lead to impracticability in 'iddah.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Take a woman who sees 10 days blood, then 2 years purity, then continuous bleeding. According to the mufta bihi (2-month max tuhr), her habit would be established based on the 2 months of purity (or less if blood appears earlier). Use this to determine her 'iddah or when she prays.
Contrast with the no-limit view: she would pray for years and have a very long 'iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأقل الطهر خمسة عشرة يوماً ولا حد لأكثره إلا لنصب العادة، فإنَّ أكثر الطهر مقدّر في حقه، ثم اختلفوا في تقدير مدتِه، والأصح أنه مقدر بستة أشهر ثم يوم الطهر. واعترض هاهنا بأنّ هذا مخالف لما مرَّ بحثه، فإنّه يعلم منه أنّ الكلّ حيض في مثل هذه الصور ؛ لعدم كون الطهر الفاصل خمسة عشر يوماً. وأجيب عنه بأن البحث السابق في المبتدأة، وهذه في المعتادة وبأنَّ الأول هو ما اختاره الجمهور، وهذه رواية عن البعض. [١] قوله : ولا حد لأكثره ؛ أي ليس له حد مقدَّرٌ شرعاً لا يزيد عليه، فتصوم وتصلي ما دامت ترى الطهر، وإن استغرقها العمر، فإنّه قد يمتد إلى سنة وسنتين، وإلى أزيد من ذلك. [٢] قوله : إلا لنصب ؛ يعني ليس لأكثر الطهر حد مقدر إلا لإقامة العادة المحتاج إليها في باب العدة، وغيرها، فإنّ الجمهور على أن أكثره مقدر. (٣) قوله : والأصح ...الخ ؛ قال العيني في «البناية شرح الهداية»: «احتيج إلى نصب العادة عند استمرار الدم عند عامة العلماء خلافاً لأبي عصمة وأبي خازم القاضي، فإنّه لا غاية لأكثره عندهما على الإطلاق ؛ لأن نصب المقادير بالسماع، ولا سماع هاهنا. وعلى هذا إذا بلغت المرأة فرأت عشرة دماً وسنة أو سنتين طهراً ثم استمر بها الدم فعندهما طهرها ما رأت، وحيضها عشرة أيام، تدعُ الصلاة في أوّل زمان الاستمرار عشرة أيام، وتصلي سنة أو سنتين، فإن طلقها زوجها تنقضي عدتها بثلاث سنين أو ست سنين اوثلاثين يوماً. وأما العامة فقد اختلفوا : فقال محمد بن شجاع : طهرها تسعة عشر يوماً ؛ لأنَّ أكثر الحيض في كلِّ شهر عشرة، والباقي تسعة عشر بيقين. وقال محمد بن سلمة : طهرها سبعة وعشرون يوماً؛ لأنَّ أقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام فما دونها، فيرفع من كل شهر فيبقى سبعة وعشرون. وقال محمد بن إبراهيم الميداني : طهرها ستة أشهر إلا ساعة، وعليه الأكثر. وقال الحاكم الشهيد : طهرها شهران وهو رواية محمد بن سماعة عن محمد؛ لأنَّ الحيض والطهر ما يتكرران في الشهر مرَّتين عادةً؛ إذ الغالب أنّ النساء يحضن في كل شهر مرة، فإذا طهرت شهرين فقد طهرتُ في أيام عادتها، فصار ذلك الطهر عادة لها، فوجب التقدير به، وهو اختيار أبي سهل، قال الإمام برهان الدين : الفتوى عليه؛ لأنه أيسر على المفتي والنساء».
The minimum period of purity is fifteen days, and there is no limit to its maximum except for establishing a habit; then the maximum purity is limited in her case. Then they differed in estimating its duration, and the most correct is that it is limited to six months plus one day of purity. There was an objection here that this contradicts what was previously discussed, because it implies that all blood is menstruation in such situations due to the absence of a separating purity of fifteen days. It was answered that the previous discussion was about the beginner, while this is about the one with a habit, and that the first view is what the majority chose, while this is a transmission from some. [1] His statement: 'and there is no limit to its maximum' means there is no fixed limit by Shari'ah beyond which it cannot increase; so she fasts and prays as long as she sees purity, even if it occupies her entire life, for it may extend to a year or two years or more. [2] His statement: 'except for establishing a habit' means that the maximum purity has no fixed limit except for establishing the habit needed in the chapter of 'iddah and others; indeed the majority hold that it is limited. (3) His statement: 'and the most correct' etc.; al-'Ayni said in al-Binaya: 'There was a need for establishing the habit when blood continues according to the general scholars, contrary to Abu 'Isma and Abu Khazim al-Qadi, for there is no limit to its maximum in their view absolutely, because establishing limits is by hearing, and there is no hearing here. Accordingly, if a woman reaches puberty and sees ten days of blood and a year or two years of purity then blood continues, then in their view her purity is what she saw, and her menstruation is ten days. She abandons prayer at the beginning of the continuation for ten days, and prays for a year or two years. If her husband divorces her, her 'iddah expires after three years or six years or thirty days.' As for the general scholars, they differed: Muhammad ibn Shuja' said: her purity is nineteen days, because the maximum menstruation in each month is ten, and what remains is nineteen with certainty. Muhammad ibn Salama said: her purity is twenty-seven days, because the minimum menstruation is three days or less; so it is removed from each month, leaving twenty-seven. Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Maidani said: her purity is six months minus one hour, and upon this is the majority. Al-Hakim al-Shahid said: her purity is two months – it is a transmission from Muhammad ibn Sama'a from Muhammad – because menstruation and purity occur twice in a month by habit, since generally women menstruate once a month; so when she is pure for two months, she has been pure during her habit days, so that purity becomes a habit for her, so estimation by it is necessary. This is the choice of Abu Sahl. Imam Burhan al-Din said: 'The fatwa is upon it, because it is easier for the mufti and the women.'
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn from Al-Wiqayah regarding the minimum and maximum durations of purity (tuhr) between menstruations. The matn states: 'The minimum purity is fifteen days, and there is no limit to its maximum.' The commentator explains that this unlimited maximum applies generally to a woman who sees purity for an extended period; she continues her prayers and fasting without restriction. However, an exception is made when a woman needs to establish a habit (nasm al-'adah) for legal purposes such as 'iddah. In that case, the maximum purity is limited, and the scholars differed on its exact duration. The preferred view (al-ashah) is that it is limited to six months minus a moment (or plus one day, as mentioned). This view is attributed to the majority (al-jumhur) from one narration. An objection is raised that this seems to contradict a previous discussion about the beginner (mubtadi'ah), where all blood is considered menstruation if the separating purity is less than fifteen days. The reply distinguishes between the beginner and the one with a habit (mu'tadah), and notes that two different reports exist from the school. Then the commentary expands from al-'Ayni's Binayah, presenting the opinions of Abu 'Isma and Abu Khazim who hold there is no limit at all, and the view of other early Hanafi scholars like Muhammad ibn Shuja' (19 days), Muhammad ibn Salama (27 days), al-Hakim al-Shahid (2 months), and al-Maidani (6 months). The passage concludes that the fatwa is on the opinion of al-Hakim al-Shahid (2 months) because it is easier for the mufti and the women.

Key Terms
الطهر (al-tuhr): The period of purity between two menstruations.
نصب العادة (nasb al-‘ādah): Establishing a habitual pattern for a woman to determine her menstruation and purity durations, especially for 'iddah.
المبتدأة (al-mubtadi'ah): A woman who experiences menstruation for the first time.
المعتادة (al-mu'tādah): A woman who has an established habit of menstruation and purity durations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason that Imam Burhan al-Din al-Marghinani chose the 2-month opinion for the fatwa?
A) It is the view of the majority of early scholars
B) It is easier for the mufti and the women
C) It is the most sound (al-ashah) according to al-'Ayni
D) It is based on a hadith from the Prophet

Key Takeaway
When establishing a habit for a woman with continuous bleeding, the Hanafi mufta bihi opinion sets the maximum tuhr at 2 months, chosen for its ease despite the existence of other views.

Estimating the Purity Period for a Mustahadah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on estimating purity period for irregular bleeding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi opinions on estimating the tuhr for a mustahadah with no known habit and explain why the two-month view is chosen for fatwa.

Core Idea
When a woman's bleeding becomes irregular with no known habit, Hanafi scholars disagree on her purity period — the easier two-month view is preferred for fatwa.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the differing estimates of the purity period (tuhr) for a mustahadah (woman with irregular bleeding) who has no known menstrual habit. Four Imams of the Hanafi school offer distinct calculations based on different principles: certainty, minima, pregnancy, and habitual recurrence. The fatwa ultimately settles on the two-month view for its ease.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Problem
Recognize the scenario: a woman with no known habit experiences continuous bleeding after a period of normal cycles. The tuhr must be estimated to determine her prayer and 'iddah obligations.
The dispute arises because the woman lacks a habitual pattern to rely on.
2
Identify the Four Views
List each scholar's estimate (Muhammad ibn Shuja': 19 days; Muhammad ibn Salamah: 27 days; Al-Maidani: 6 months minus 1 hour; Al-Hakim al-Shahid: 2 months) and note the reasoning behind each.
Each view uses a different basis: max menses, min menses, shortest pregnancy, or habitual recurrence.
3
Analyze the Tarjih
Why was the two-month view chosen? Because it relies on the habitual recurrence of menses (once a month) and is easier for mufti and women to apply.
Imam Burhan al-Din explicitly states the fatwa is upon this view.
4
Apply to 'Iddah
The estimated tuhr directly impacts the 'iddah period for a mustahadah. Under the fatwa view, if she divorces, her 'iddah would be calculated based on two-month tuhr cycles.
Different views would yield different 'iddah durations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا إذا بلغت المرأة فرأت عشرة دماً وسنة أو سنتين طهراً ثم استمر بها الدم فعندهما طهرها ما رأت، وحيضها عشرة أيام، تدعُ الصلاة في أوّل زمان الاستمرار عشرة أيام، وتصلي سنة أو سنتين، فإن طلقها زوجها تنقضي عدتها بثلاث سنين أو ست سنين اوثلاثين يوماً. وأما العامة فقد اختلفوا : فقال محمد بن شجاع الله : طهرها تسعة عشر يوماً؛ لأنَّ أكثر الحيض في كلِّ شهر عشرة، والباقي تسعة عشر بيقين. وقال محمد بن سلمة : طهرها سبعة وعشرون يوماً؛ لأنَّ أقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام فما دونها، فيرفع من كل شهر فيبقى سبعة وعشرون. وقال محمد بن إبراهيم الميداني : طهرها ستة أشهر إلا ساعة، وعليه الأكثر. وقال الحاكم الشهيد : طهرها شهران وهو رواية محمد بن سماعة عن محمد؛ لأنَّ الحيض والطهر ما يتكرّران في الشهر مرتين عادة؛ إذ الغالب أنّ النساء يحضن في كل شهر مرة، فإذا طهرت شهرين فقد طهرت في أيام عادتها، فصار ذلك الطهر عادة لها، فوجب التقدير به، وهو اختيار أبي سهل، قال الإمام برهان الدين : الفتوى عليه؛ لأنه أيسر على المفتي والنساء. انتهى
And upon this, if a woman reaches maturity and sees ten days of blood and one or two years of purity, then the blood continues in her, then according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) her purity is what she saw, and her menses is ten days. She abandons prayer at the first time of continuation for ten days, and she prays for one or two years. If her husband divorces her, her waiting period ends with three years or six years or thirty days. As for the general scholars, they differed: Muhammad ibn Shuja' said: her purity is nineteen days because the maximum of menses in every month is ten, and the remainder is nineteen with certainty. Muhammad ibn Salamah said: her purity is twenty-seven days because the minimum of menses is three days or less, so from each month there remains twenty-seven. Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Maidani said: her purity is six months minus one hour, and the majority are upon this. Al-Hakim al-Shahid said: her purity is two months, and it is a narration of Muhammad ibn Sam'ah from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) because menstruation and purity recur in the month twice habitually, since the majority of women menstruate once a month. If she is pure for two months, then she has been pure in her customary days, so that purity becomes a habit for her, and estimation by it is binding. This is the choice of Abu Sahl. Imam Burhan al-Din said: the fatwa is upon it because it is easier for the mufti and for women. End.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the estimation of the purity period (tuhr) for a woman who has irregular bleeding (istihadah) without a known prior habit. The context begins with the scenario of a woman whose menses used to be ten days and purity one or two years, then her bleeding becomes continuous. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, her original pattern defines her menses (ten days) and her purity (the long period), so she acts accordingly for prayer and 'iddah. Then the passage turns to the 'general scholars' (al-‘ammah) who differ on how to determine the tuhr when no habit is known. Four opinions are given: (1) Muhammad ibn Shuja'— tuhr is 19 days, reasoning that since the maximum menses in a month is 10 days, the remaining days are definitely pure. (2) Muhammad ibn Salamah— tuhr is 27 days, based on the minimum menses of 3 days, so the rest is pure. (3) Al-Maidani— tuhr is 6 months minus 1 hour, invoking the principle that the shortest pregnancy is 6 months, and since a non-pregnant woman's purity is less than a pregnant one's, it is reduced by an hour. (4) Al-Hakim al-Shahid— tuhr is 2 months, transmitted from Muhammad al-Shaybani, based on the habitual recurrence of menses and purity twice in two months (since most women menstruate once a month, a two-month pure stretch becomes habitual). The author notes that al-Hakim al-Shahid's view is the choice of Abu Sahl, and Imam Burhan al-Din confirms the fatwa is on it because it is easier for the mufti and women. This dispute directly impacts the calculation of 'iddah for such women.

Key Terms
طهر (tuhr): The period of purity between two menstrual bleeding episodes.
حيض (hayḍ): Menstrual blood that is habitual and within the normal duration (min 3, max 10 days).
استحاضة (istiḥāḍah): Irregular bleeding that is not menstrual; it is caused by illness or a vein.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the view of Al-Hakim al-Shahid, what is the estimated purity period (tuhr) for a mustahadah with no known habit?
A) 19 days
B) 6 months minus 1 hour
C) 2 months
D) 27 days

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa settles on a two-month purity period for a mustahadah with no known habit, based on habitual recurrence and ease for mufti and women.

Categories of Istihadah and Their Proof
Defining istihadah via bounds of menses and nifas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi categories of istihadah and apply the hadith proof to identify when bleeding is istihadah rather than menses.

Core Idea
Blood outside the Hanafi bounds of menses (3–10 days) or exceeding habit beyond the maximum is istihadah, confirmed by the mustahadah hadith.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies which types of uterine bleeding are considered istihadah (irregular) rather than menses (hayd) or postpartum (nifas). Understanding these boundaries is essential for determining a woman's religious obligations such as prayer, fasting, and 'iddah. The text relies on a famous hadith of the mustahadah to separate habit-based menses from irregular bleeding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of istihadah
The text lists three types of blood that are considered istihadah: (1) blood less than 3 days, (2) blood exceeding 10 days (for menses) or 40 days (for nifas), and (3) blood exceeding a woman's habit beyond the maximum days. Note the exception of one hour in the mubtadi'ah case.
The 'exception of an hour' relates to calculating the habit of purity between menses.
2
Understand the linguistic and logical reasoning
Blood from the vagina is only three types: hayd, nifas, istihadah. Blood less than 3 days cannot be hayd (by hadith), nor nifas (as it occurs in non-postpartum context), so it must be istihadah. The same applies to blood exceeding 10 or 40 days.
The minimum of hayd is established by ahadith, such as the one stating 'the minimum of menses is three days and three nights.'
3
Examine the hadith proof for habit-based boundaries
The hadith of the mustahadah states: 'She leaves prayer during the days of her menses (aqra'), then she bathes and prays.' This indicates that the habit (the time of usual menses) is considered menstrual, while blood beyond the habit (but within 10 days) is still menses if it does not exceed the maximum? Actually, the hadith is used to show that blood outside the habit is not menses when it exceeds the maximum.
The hadith is reported by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, and Darqutni.
4
Apply the rules to a concrete case
The text gives a case: a mubtadi'ah sees 10 days blood then 6 months purity, then continuous bleeding. Her 'iddah is 19 months minus 3 hours. Work through the calculation: 3 menses (each 10 days) + 3 purities (each 6 months minus 1 hour).
The 'hour' adjustment accounts for the fact that purity of a non-pregnant woman is shorter than that of a pregnant woman by an hour.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما نقص عن أقل الحيض أو زاد على أكثره أو على عادة عرفت لحيض وجاوز العشرة أو نفاس وجاوز الأربعين إلا ساعة؛ لأن العادة نقصان طهر غير الحامل عن طهر الحامل، وأقل مدة الحمل ستة أشهر، فانتقص عن هذا بشيء، وهو الساعة، صورته: مبتدأة رأت عشرة أيام دماً وستة أشهر طهراً ثم استمر الدم تنقضي عدتها بتسعة عشر شهراً إلا ثلاث ساعات؛ لأنا نحتاج إلى ثلاث حيض كل حيض عشرة أيام وإلى ثلاثة أطهار كل طهر ستة أشهر إلا ساعة. [2] قوله: عن أقل الحيض... الخ؛ لما فرغ عن ذكر أحكام الحيض والنفاس شرع في ذكر الاستحاضة وأحكامها وذكر في أقسامها أقساماً عديدة، منها: الدم الناقص عن أقل الحيض، وهو الدم الذي لم يبلغ ثلاثة أيام ولياليها؛ وذلك لأنه لما ثبت بالأحاديث أن أقل الحيض هو المقدار المذكور. ومن المعلوم أن الدماء الخارجة من فرج المرأة ليست إلا ثلاثة: حيض، ونفاس، واستحاضة، وهذا ليس بنفاس قطعاً، ولا حيضاً؛ لكونه أقل من مدة تقررت شرعاً للحيض، فلا بد أن يكون استحاضة، وقس عليه ما زاد على أكثر الحيض أو أكثر النفاس. [2] قوله: أو على؛ عطف على قوله: «على أكثره»، يعني الدم الذي زاد على عادة كانت مقررة لحيض، ومع ذلك جاوز العشرة، وكذا الدم الزائد على عادة عرفت لنفاس، ومع ذلك جاوز أكثر مدته وهو أربعون يوماً، وذلك لحديث: «المستحاضة تدع الصلاة أيام أقرائها ثم تغتسل وتصلي»، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي وابن ماجة والدارقطني وغيرهم.
And what is less than the minimum of menses or exceeds its maximum, or exceeds a habit known for menses and exceeds ten days, or for postpartum and exceeds forty days except an hour; because the habit is that the purity of a non-pregnant woman is less than that of a pregnant woman, and the minimum duration of pregnancy is six months, so it has been decreased from this by something, which is an hour. Its form: a woman without prior menses sees ten days of blood and six months of purity, then blood continues; her waiting period ends after nineteen months minus three hours, because we need three menses, each menses ten days, and three purities, each purity six months minus an hour. [2] His saying: 'less than the minimum of menses' — when he finished mentioning the rulings of menses and postpartum, he began to mention istihadah and its rulings, and mentioned among its categories numerous categories, including: blood less than the minimum of menses, which is blood that has not reached three days and their nights; because it has been established in hadith that the minimum of menses is the mentioned amount. It is known that the bloods coming out of a woman's vagina are only three: menses, postpartum, and istihadah. This is definitely not postpartum, nor menses, because it is less than a duration determined legally for menses, so it must be istihadah. And analogize to it what exceeds the maximum of menses or maximum of postpartum. [2] His saying: 'or exceeds' — it is conjuncted to his saying 'on its maximum', meaning the blood that exceeds a habit that was established for menses, and despite that it exceeds ten days, and likewise blood that exceeds a habit known for postpartum, and despite that it exceeds its maximum duration of forty days. And that is due to the hadith: 'The mustahadah leaves prayer during her menses days, then she bathes and prays.' Reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, al-Darqutni and others.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that defines the categories of istihadah (irregular bleeding). It begins by stating that blood which is less than the minimum menses (3 days) or more than the maximum (10 days), or blood that exceeds a woman's customary habit for menses or postpartum (nifas) beyond the maximum days (10 for menses, 40 for nifas), is considered istihadah, with an exception of one hour ('illa sa'a) for the case of a mubtadi'ah (a woman who has no prior menses experience) as explained. The reasoning: the minimum duration of pregnancy is six months, and a non-pregnant woman's purity is shorter by an hour. An example is given: a mubtadi'ah sees 10 days blood, then 6 months purity, then continuous bleeding. Her 'iddah (waiting period after divorce) is calculated as 19 months minus 3 hours (3 menses of 10 days each plus 3 purities of 6 months minus 1 hour each). Then the commentary expands: the author explains that after discussing menses and postpartum, the text turns to istihadah. The first category is blood less than 3 days; this cannot be menses or postpartum, so it must be istihadah. The same applies to blood exceeding 10 days or 40 days. The key proof is the hadith of the mustahadah: 'She leaves prayer during the days of her menses, then she bathes and prays.' This indicates that the blood beyond her habit is not menses but istihadah, and she should pray after the habit days end.

Key Terms
أقل الحيض (aqall al-ḥayḍ): The minimum duration of menstruation, which is three days in the Hanafi school.
استحاضة (istiḥāḍah): Irregular bleeding that is not menstrual or postpartum; it is blood from a vein (istihadah) that does not have the rulings of hayd.
عادة (ʿādah): The customary pattern of a woman's menses in duration and timing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, istihadah is blood that falls outside the 3-10 day range for menses or outside the habit but exceeding the maximum, confirmed by the mustahadah hadith.

Categories of Istihadah: Blood Outside Hayd and Nifas Limits
Classifying blood as istihadah when below minimum, above maximum, or exceeding habit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any blood discharge as hayd, nifas, or istihadah by applying the Hanafi thresholds and the concept of habit (ʿādah).

Core Idea
Blood becomes istihadah only if it falls below the minimum, exceeds the maximum, or surpasses habit beyond the maximum.

In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi school distinguishes istihadah from normal menstruation and postpartum bleeding. The key is knowing the fixed limits (minimum 3 days for hayd, maximum 10 days for hayd and 40 for nifas) and understanding the role of a woman's established habit. We will also see how a novice (mubtada'ah) differs from a habitual woman (mu'tadah) in classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's type
Determine if she is a habitual woman (mu'tadah) with a known pattern or a novice (mubtada'ah) experiencing bleeding for the first time. This decides which rule applies.
2
Check the blood duration against the fixed limits
For both types: if blood lasts less than 3 days (minimum hayd) or exceeds 10 days (maximum hayd) or exceeds 40 days (maximum nifas), it is istihadah. But for a habitual woman, habit matters first.
3
Apply the habit rule for mu'tadah
If her blood duration is within her habit (e.g., 5 days of a 7-day habit) and does not exceed the maximum (10 or 40), it is hayd/nifas. If it exceeds habit but still within maximum, it is still hayd/nifas because the habit may change. If it exceeds both habit and maximum, the excess beyond maximum is istihadah.
Example: habit 7 days, sees 12 days → first 7 are hayd, last 5 are istihadah (since 12>10).
4
Apply the rule for mubtada'ah
A novice's blood is considered hayd/nifas up to the maximum (10 days hayd, 40 nifas). Only if it exceeds that maximum does it become istihadah. Her initial blood is not ruled istihadah even if it is less than 3 days? Actually, the passage says blood less than 3 days is istihadah even for a novice? The text: 'blood less than the minimum... it must be istihadah' – this applies to all women. So a novice seeing blood for 1 day is istihadah because it is less than the minimum. The rule for novice is mainly about when blood exceeds habit? She has no habit, so we rely on the maxima. The passage: 'the novice... if blood continues after first delivery, what exceeds 40 is istihadah, less than that is nifas' and 'the novice's blood at the beginning of puberty is not istihadah until it exceeds 10 days.' So for hayd, if she sees 5 days, it's hayd; if 15 days, first 10 hayd, last 5 istihadah. But if she sees 2 days, that's less than minimum, so it's istihadah. The passage doesn't explicitly state that last case, but logically it applies. I'll clarify in explanation.

وما نقص عن أقل الحيض أو زاد على أكثره أو على عادةٍ عُرِفَت لحيض، وجاوز العشرة، أو نفاس وجاوز الأربعين إلا ساعة ؛ لأنَّ العادة نقصانُ طُهْرِ غير الحامل عن طُهر الحامل، وأقل مدة الحمل ستة أشهر، فانتقص عن هذا بشيء، وهو الساعة، صورتُه : مبتدأة رأت عشرة أيام دَماً، وستة أشهر طهراً، ثُمَّ استمرَ الدَّمُ تنقضي عدتها بتسعة عشر شهراً إلا ثلاث ساعات ؛ لأنا نحتاج إلى ثلاث حيض، كُل حيض عشرة أيام، وإلى ثلاثة أطهار، كلُّ طُهر ستة أشهر إلا ساعة.

(وما نقص عن أقل الحيض ) : أي الدَّمُ النَّاقصُ عن الثلاثة، أو زاد على أكثره) : أي على العشرة (أو أكثر النفاس)، وهو أربعون يوماً، (أو على عادة عُرفت الحيض، وجاوز العشرة، أو نفاس وجاوز الأربعين) : أي إذا كانت لها وهناك أقوال أخر أيضاً مَن شاء الاطلاع عليها مع ما لها وما عليها فليطالع «السعاية».

A habit known in menstruation—we suppose it is seven days, for example—she sees blood for twelve days, so the five days after the seven are istihadah. [2] His statement: 'this' refers to what he mentioned by his saying 'or on a habit known' etc. [3] His statement: 'the novice' is she who had not reached puberty before that, and her blood came for the first time, and it is in the passive form; and it is said in the active form, and the more famous and apparent is the first. [4] His statement: 'who reaches puberty while in a state of istihadah' means she becomes an adult while being considered in a state of istihadah, so this is of the category of a circumstantial clause meaning 'being considered,' like His statement: 'So enter it to dwell eternally,' i.e., being considered eternal, for eternity is not at the time of entry. Similarly, here, it is not ruled that the blood of a novice at the beginning of her puberty is istihadah; rather, that is established by its exceeding ten days. [0] His statement: 'or on forty of her postpartum bleeding' means the postpartum of a novice: she who has not given birth before, so if blood continues after her first delivery, what exceeds forty is istihadah, and what is less than that is postpartum.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of blood that falls outside the normal limits of menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) as istihadah (chronic non-menstrual bleeding). The author first states the rule: any blood less than the minimum hayd (three days and nights) or more than the maximum (ten days) or exceeding a woman's habit while surpassing ten days (for hayd) or forty days (for nifas) is istihadah. The exception 'except an hour' refers to a specific calculation for the 'iddah of a novice woman who has a pattern of ten days blood and six months purity; her 'iddah is nineteen months minus three hours. The commentary then elaborates: the minimum hayd is established by hadith, and since blood from a woman is only hayd, nifas, or istihadah, blood falling short of the minimum or exceeding the maximum must be istihadah. For a woman with a habit (mu'tadah), if she sees blood beyond her habit but still within the maximum (ten days for hayd, forty for nifas), it is considered hayd/nifas because the habit may change. But if the blood exceeds the maximum, the entire excess is istihadah. The author then discusses the novice (mubtada'ah): a girl who reaches puberty and sees blood for the first time; her initial blood is not istihadah until it exceeds ten days. Similarly, for postpartum, a novice's blood up to forty days is nifas; beyond that is istihadah. The hadith from Abu Dawud et al. is cited: the mustahadah leaves prayer during her usual menstrual days then performs ghusl and prays, indicating that days beyond habit are istihadah. The commentary also clarifies linguistic points: 'al-mubtada'ah' is the passive form, meaning she has blood for the first time. Overall, this passage establishes clear criteria to distinguish pathological bleeding from normal menstruation and lochia, a critical ruling for women's worship and legal purity.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstrual blood; minimum 3 days, maximum 10 days.
النفاس (al-nifas): Postpartum bleeding; maximum 40 days; no minimum specified.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Chronic non-menstrual bleeding from the womb due to a vein rupture or other cause; it is not hayd or nifas.
المعتادة (al-mu'tadah): A woman who has an established habit (customary duration) of menses or postpartum bleeding.
المبتدأة (al-mubtada'ah): A woman who experiences menses or postpartum for the first time, having no prior habit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A novice woman (mubtada'ah) sees blood for 9 days. According to the Hanafi school, this blood is:
A) Istihadah, because it exceeds 3 days?
B) Hayd, because it does not exceed the maximum of 10 days.
C) Istihadah, because she is a novice and any bleeding is istihadah until proven otherwise.
D) Hayd, only if it is less than 3 days.

Key Takeaway
Blood is classified as istihadah only when it falls below the minimum, exceeds the maximum, or surpasses a known habit beyond the maximum; the habit of a mu'tadah is respected within the maximum, while a mubtada'ah's first bleeding is hayd/nifas up to the maximum.

Ruling for a Habitual Woman: Exceeding Habit but Within Maximum
States ruling for a habitual woman with blood exceeding habit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a habitual woman (mu'tada) when her blood exceeds her habit, and distinguish when the excess is considered istihadah versus when it may still be hayd.

Core Idea
Blood beyond habit is not automatically istihadah—it becomes istihadah only when it exceeds the maximum duration of hayd or nifas.

This lesson explains the Hanafi classification of menstrual (hayd) and chronic (istihadah) blood for a woman with an established habit. The key principle is that a change in habit is possible, so blood beyond habit but within the maximum period is treated as hayd unless it exceeds the maximum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's status
Determine if the woman has a known habit (mu'tada) in hayd or nifas. A habit is established after at least one previous occurrence.
The habit includes both duration and timing.
2
Compare blood duration to habit and maximum
If the current bleeding exceeds the habit but does not exceed the maximum (10 days for hayd, 40 for nifas), it is not automatically istihadah. The possibility of habit change remains.
The maximum duration is the absolute limit for hayd/nifas according to the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the rule: within maximum = potential hayd
If bleeding exceeds habit but is still within the maximum, the excess may be hayd. The woman should act according to her habit until the blood surpasses the maximum.
Example: habit 7 days, sees blood for 9 days – all 9 are hayd because 9 < 10 (max).
4
Apply the rule: exceeding maximum = istihadah
If the bleeding continues beyond the maximum duration, the entire amount beyond the habit is considered istihadah. The habit is no longer valid for this cycle.
Example: habit 7 days, sees blood for 12 days – first 7 are hayd, days 8-12 are istihadah.
5
Understand the rationale
The possibility of habit change (tabaddul al-'adah) is considered within the maximum period. Only when the maximum is exceeded is it clear that the extra blood is not hayd.
This ruling is based on multiple hadiths indicating that what exceeds the habit is istihadah, but only when also exceeding the maximum.

Source Text

أو على عشرة حيض مَن  َبلَغَتْ مستحاضة، أو على أربعين نفاسها عادة معروفة في الحيض، وفرضناها سبعةً مثلاً، فرأت الدم اثني عشر يوماً، فخمسة أيام أيام بعد السبعة استحاضة، وإذا كانت لها عادة في النفاس، وهي ثلاثون يوماً مثلاً، فرأت الدم خمسين يوماً، فالعشرون التي بعد الثلاثين استحاضة، هذا حُكْمُ المعتادة. وفي الباب أحاديث كثيرة تدلّ على أنّ ما زاد على العادة استحاضة، وإنما اشترط كونه مجاوزاً على أكثر مدة الحيض والنفاس ؛ لأنه إذا زاد على أيام العادة ولم يزد على أكثر المدة لا يحكم بكونه استحاضة ؛ لبقاء المدة، واحتمال تبدل العادة في هذه المرة، وإذا استمر إلى أن جاوز أكثر المدَّة عُلِمَ أنَّ الكلَّ مما زاد على العادة وكان في المدة وما زادَ عليها كله استحاضة. [1]قوله : فرأت الدم اثني عشر يوماً ؛ فإن رأت الدم عشرة أيام يكون كله حيضاً لبقاء المدة واحتمال تبدل العادة. [٢]قوله : هذا ؛ أي الذي ذكره بقوله : «أو على عادة عرفت»... الخ.
Or on ten [days] of menstruation for one who reached puberty with istihadah, or on forty [days] of her postpartum bleeding, with a known habit in menstruation. We suppose it to be seven, for example; if she sees blood for twelve days, then five days after the seven are istihadah. And if she has a habit in postpartum bleeding, it being thirty days for example, and she sees blood for fifty days, then the twenty after thirty are istihadah. This is the ruling for the habitual woman. And in this chapter there are many hadiths indicating that what exceeds the habit is istihadah, and the condition of exceeding the maximum duration of hayd and nifas is stipulated; because if it exceeds the days of the habit but does not exceed the maximum duration, it is not ruled as istihadah due to the remaining time and the possibility of a change in habit this time. If it continues until it exceeds the maximum duration, it is known that all of it, from what exceeded the habit and was within the duration and beyond, is istihadah. [1] His saying: she saw blood for twelve days; if she sees blood for ten days, all of it is hayd due to the remaining time and the possibility of a change in habit. Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining the case of a woman with a known habit (mu'tada). The author begins by stating the rule: a woman who reaches puberty with istihadah (metrorrhagia) or a postpartum woman whose blood exceeds her known habit is treated as having istihadah for the excess, provided the total blood exceeds the maximum duration of hayd (10 days) or nifas (40 days). Examples are given: if a woman's habit is 7 days but she bleeds 12 days, the first 7 are hayd and the next 5 are istihadah. Similarly for nifas with a 30-day habit and 50-day bleeding. The commentary notes that as long as the bleeding does not exceed the maximum duration (10 for hayd, 40 for nifas), it could still be hayd due to the possibility of habit change. Only when it exceeds the maximum is it definitively istihadah. The proof from many hadith is alluded to but not quoted.

Key Terms
المعتادة (al-mu'tada): A woman who has a known habit (ada) in the duration and timing of her menstrual or postpartum bleeding.
استحاضة (istihadah): Chronic vaginal bleeding that is not menstrual or postpartum, but due to a vein rupture. The rulings of hayd do not apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
For a habitual woman, blood beyond the habit is istihadah only when it also exceeds the maximum duration; within the maximum, a change of habit is possible.

Ruling for a Beginner (Mubtadi'a) in Hayd and Nifas
Defines and rules for a woman with no prior habit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a mubtadi'a, state the default rulings for her hayd and nifas, and explain how a habit is established according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A mubtadi'a's hayd defaults to 10 days and nifas to 40 days until a habit is established.

In this passage, the author clarifies the rulings for a woman with no prior menstrual or postpartum habit (mubtadi'a). Understanding this is crucial because many women face this situation at the onset of puberty or first childbirth. We will explore why the maximum durations are applied and how a habit becomes established within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Determine who is a mubtadi'a: a woman who has no established habit of hayd or nifas because she has just reached puberty or given birth for the first time.
2
State the default rulings
For hayd, the default is 10 days per month. For nifas, the default is 40 days. Any blood beyond these maxima is istihadah.
3
Understand the reasoning
The maximum is used because certainty of hayd is given priority over doubt; since no habit exists, the days are suitable for hayd up to the maximum.
4
Note the intra-school difference on habit establishment
Abu Hanifa holds that one occurrence establishes a habit; the Sahibayn require two occurrences. The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view.
Refer to Al-Jami' al-Rumuz for the tarjih.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a mubtadi'a with continuous blood, classify the first 10 days (or 40 for nifas) as hayd/nifas and the rest as istihadah until a habit is formed.

[2] فقوله : حيض من بلغت بالجر عطف بيان لعشرة، وقولُه : نفاسها بالجر عطف بيان لأربعين. [1] قوله : عشرة أيام ؛ وذلك لأنه لما لم تكن لها عادة معروفة حتى يُرَدُّ إليه أمرُ حيضها يعتبر أكثر مدة الحيض ؛ لأنَّ دخولها في الحيض متيقن، والأيام صالحة له، فلا يحكم بخروجها عنه بالشك، وما زاد على أكثر المدة يكون استحاضة لا محالة ؛ لعدم صلوح الأيام للحيض. [٣]قوله : عادة ; لمدة قال في «جامع الرموز»: «اعلم أنّ المدة تصير عادة عند الطرفين بمرتين ; لأنها مشتقة من العود وعنده بمرة، وعليه الفتوى كما هو المشهور ; إذ المراهقة إذا رأت مدة واحدة منها صارت عادة لها، فلو رأت مرَّتين أو أكثر، ثم استمر بها الدم رُدَّت إلى العادةِ المتكرّرة عندهما ، وإلى آخر ما رأت عنده»(۱). [٤]قوله : بالجر ; ويمكن أن يكون بالرفع خبر مبتدأ محذوف، ويكون العشرة منوناً، وقس عليه قوله : نفاسها. [0] قوله : أو على أربعين نفاسها ; أي نفاس المبتدأة : وهي التي لم تلد قبل، فإذا استمر بها الدم بعد أوّل ولادتها فما زاد على أربعين استحاضة، وما دونه نفاس.
Then he intended to explain the ruling of the beginner (mubtadi'a), so he said: (Or on ten [days] of menstruation for one who reached puberty with istihadah, or on forty [days] of her postpartum bleeding). The beginner who reached puberty with istihadah: her menstruation is ten days from each month, and what exceeds it is istihadah, so her purity is twenty days. As for postpartum bleeding, if the woman does not have a known habit in it, her postpartum period is forty days, and what exceeds it is istihadah. [2] So his saying: 'haydi man balaghat' is in the genitive as an explanatory apposition to 'ten', and his saying: 'nifasuha' is in the genitive as an explanatory apposition to 'forty'. [1] His saying: ten days; that is because since she does not have a known habit to which the matter of her menstruation is referred, the maximum duration of menstruation is considered, because her entry into menstruation is certain, and the days are suitable for it, so it is not ruled that she exits from it by doubt. What exceeds the maximum duration is definitely istihadah due to the unsuitability of the days for menstruation. [2] His saying: twenty days; based on the fact that the majority of women menstruate once every month. [3] His saying: habit; Al-Jami' al-Rumuz said: 'Know that the duration becomes a habit according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) with two occurrences, because it is derived from 'return' (al-'awd), and according to him (Abu Hanifa) with one occurrence, and the fatwa is upon that as is well-known; because if a girl nearing puberty sees one duration, it becomes her habit. If she sees two or more, then her blood continues, she is referred to the repeated habit according to them, and to the last she saw according to him.' [4] His saying: in the genitive; and it is possible that it is in the nominative as the predicate of an omitted subject, and 'ten' is nunated, and similarly his saying: 'nifasuha'. [0] His saying: or on forty [days] of her postpartum bleeding; i.e., the postpartum bleeding of the beginner: she who has not given birth before. If blood continues after her first delivery, what exceeds forty is istihadah, and what is less is nifas.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling for a woman with no previous menstrual or postpartum history (mubtadi'a). The author states that her hayd is set at 10 days per month, and her nifas at 40 days; any blood beyond those maxima is istihadah. The commentary explains why the maximum duration is used: since there is no habit to rely on, the default is the maximum possible hayd (10 days) because certainty of hayd is given priority over doubt of istihadah. The purity period is calculated as 20 days based on the norm of one menstruation per month. The commentary also discusses how a habit is established: according to Abu Hanifa, one occurrence suffices; according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), two occurrences are needed. The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view. The grammatical analysis of the phrase is also noted.

Key Terms
المبتدأة (al-mubtadi'a): A woman who has no established habit of hayd or nifas because she has just reached puberty or given birth for the first time.
العادة (al-'ada): A recurring duration of hayd or nifas that becomes a reference for the woman's rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 8 days, all hayd
B) 10 days, assuming it continues to 10
C) 8 days, but if it exceeds 10, then 10 are hayd
D) 8 days, but if it exceeds 10, the first 10 are hayd

Key Takeaway
For a mubtadi'a, hayd is a maximum of 10 days and nifas 40 days until a habit is established, with the fatwa that habit is established by one occurrence.

Ruling on Blood Seen by a Pregnant Woman: Istihadah, Not Hayd
States the ruling with causal explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a pregnant woman's bleeding, explain the reasoning (illa) of cervical closure, and apply the practical consequences for worship.

Core Idea
A pregnant woman's blood is istihadah, not hayd — the closed cervix prevents menstrual blood.

This passage from the Hanafi text clearly establishes that any blood a pregnant woman sees is classified as istihadah (chronic non-menstrual bleeding). The ruling is based on the physiological cause that the cervix is closed during pregnancy, making it impossible for menstrual blood to exit. Understanding this ruling is essential for a pregnant woman to know whether she must continue her prayers, fasting, and marital relations.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Ruling
Read the statement: 'Or what a pregnant woman sees is istihadah.' Recognize that this is a definitive ruling that the blood is not hayd.
2
Identify the Causal Reasoning (Illa)
Find the reason given in the commentary: 'because the mouth of the uterus is closed during pregnancy.' This is the cause that differentiates the blood from menstrual bleeding.
The illa is anatomical – cervical closure.
3
Derive the Practical Implications
Since the blood is istihadah, the woman remains in a state of purity for the purposes of prayer, fasting, and marital relations. She only needs to perform wudu for each obligatory prayer.
This is in contrast to hayd, which prohibits all these acts.
4
Situate the Ruling within the Hanafi School
Recognize that this is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position from Zahir al-Riwayah. There is no intra-Hanafi dispute on this issue.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) allow that a pregnant woman can have hayd if the blood meets the usual criteria. The Hanafi position is distinctive in its categorical ruling.
This difference has practical consequences for the woman's obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ما رأت حامل فهو استحاضة) : أي الدِّمُ الذي تراه المرأة الحامل ليس بحيض، بل هو استحاضة. [0] قوله : أو ما رأت حامل ; أي الدم الذي رأته امرأة في رحمها جنين في أيام حملها، فهذا الدم ليس من الرحم حتى يكون حيضاً ; لانسدادِ فم الرحم أيام الحمل، بل هو دم عرق انفجر، فكان استحاضة.
Or what a pregnant woman sees is istihadah: i.e., the blood that a pregnant woman sees is not hayd, but rather istihadah. [0] His saying: or what a pregnant woman sees; i.e., the blood that a woman with a fetus in her womb sees during the days of her pregnancy. This blood is not from the uterus to be hayd, because the mouth of the uterus is closed during pregnancy; rather, it is blood from a burst vein, so it is istihadah.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a clear ruling: any blood a pregnant woman experiences is considered istihadah (chronic non-menstrual bleeding), not hayd. The reasoning ('illa) is that during pregnancy, the cervix (mouth of the uterus) is closed, so menstrual blood cannot exit. The blood must be from a ruptured vein, hence it is istihadah. This ruling has practical consequences: the woman must continue praying and fasting, and intercourse is permissible (unlike during hayd). The commentary emphasizes the cause (insidad famm al-rahim) to justify the ruling.

Key Terms
حامل (hamil): A woman who is pregnant, with a fetus in her womb.
استحاضة (istihadah): Chronic vaginal bleeding from a vein, not from the uterus; not subject to hayd rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is hayd if it occurs at her usual period time
B) It is istihadah because her uterus is closed
C) It is nifas if she has given birth
D) It is considered impurities but not istihadah

Key Takeaway
A pregnant woman's blood is always istihadah in the Hanafi school, so she must fulfill all religious obligations.

Maximum Durations for Hayd and Nifas (Non-Habitual Woman)
Ruling on maximum hayd and nifas for non-habitual women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum duration of hayd (10 days) and nifas (40 days) for a woman without a known habit, and calculate the purity period (20 days).

Core Idea
Without known habit, hayd max is 10 days, nifas max 40 days; excess is istihada.

In this lesson, we examine the default durations for menstrual and postpartum bleeding when a woman has no established pattern. The Hanafi school relies on the maximum possible durations based on the norm of most women and the principle of certainty. Understanding these limits is essential for distinguishing hayd from istihada and applying the correct rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for a maximum duration
Without a known habit, we cannot refer to her own pattern, so we use the maximum possible duration for hayd (10 days) and nifas (40 days).
2
Apply the rule for hayd
Any blood up to 10 days is hayd; beyond 10 is istihada.
3
Calculate the purity period
Since most women menstruate once a month, the purity between hayd periods is calculated as 20 days (30 - 10).
This is based on the norm, not a fixed rule for every woman.
4
Apply the rule for nifas
Postpartum bleeding up to 40 days is nifas; beyond is istihada.

Book of Purification / Menstruation. 041. Or what a pregnant woman sees is istihada. Every month ten days, and what exceeds it is istihada, so her purity is twenty days. As for nifas, if the woman has no known habit regarding it, her nifas is forty days, and what exceeds it is istihada. [2] His saying: 'hayd from balaghat' in genitive as apposition to ten, and his saying: 'nifasuha' in genitive as apposition to forty. (Or what a pregnant woman sees is istihada): i.e., the blood that a pregnant woman sees is not hayd, but istihada. [1] His saying: ten days; because when she has no known habit to which her hayd matter can be referred, the maximum duration of hayd is considered; because her entry into hayd is certain, and the days are suitable for it, so one does not rule her exit from it by doubt, and what exceeds the maximum duration is necessarily istihada due to the days' unsuitability for hayd. Once. [2] His saying: twenty days; based on the fact that the majority of women menstruate once every month. [3] His saying: habit; he said in 'Jami' al-Rumuz': 'Know that the duration becomes a habit according to the two sides by two occurrences because it is derived from return, and according to him by one occurrence, and the fatwa is upon it as is well-known; for a young girl approaching puberty if she sees a single duration it becomes her habit, so if she sees two or more, then blood continues, she is referred to the repeated habit according to them, and to the last she saw according to him.' (1) End from 'Jami' al-Rumuz' (1:56). It does not prevent prayer, fasting, and intercourse, and one for whom no time of obligatory prayer passes except that he has a hadath from istihada, nosebleed, or the like, he performs wudu for the time of every obligatory prayer. His saying: 'wa ma naqsa' is mubtada, and his saying: 'fahuwa istihada' is its khabar. Then he explained the ruling of istihada, saying: (It does not prevent prayer, fasting, and intercourse, and one for whom no time of obligatory prayer passes except that he has a hadath i.e., the hadath with which he is afflicted, (from istihada, or nosebleed, or the like, performs wudu for the time of every obligatory prayer as a precaution against the saying of al-Shafi'i, for according to him he performs wudu for every prayer) and he prays supererogatory prayers following the obligatory. And it is indicated by what has been narrated in multiple reports that the Prophet, peace be upon him, forbade intercourse with pregnant captives until they give birth, and forbade intercourse with non-pregnant ones until they are free of a hayda, and that is only to know the purity of her womb from pregnancy, so he made hayd a sign of purity, so it is known that a pregnant woman does not have hayd.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn regarding hayd and istihada. It begins by stating that for a woman with no known habit, the maximum duration of hayd is ten days; any blood beyond that is istihada. The purity period between such hayds is twenty days, based on the norm that women menstruate once a month. For nifas (postpartum bleeding), the maximum is forty days if no habit is established; excess is istihada. The commentator then explains the grammatical construction of the matn: 'hayd min balaghat' is in the genitive as an apposition to 'ten', and 'nifasuha' is also genitive. For a pregnant woman, the blood she sees is not hayd but istihada, because the mouth of the womb is closed during pregnancy, so the blood is from a vein, not from the uterus. The commentator clarifies that the ten-day maximum is used because without a known habit, one relies on the maximum possible hayd duration, since we are certain she is in hayd initially and we cannot rule by doubt that she has left it. The habit formation is discussed: according to Abu Hanifa (with mufta bihi), one occurrence establishes a habit; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, two occurrences are needed. Finally, the commentator states the ruling of istihada: it does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations. For a person afflicted with a continual hadath (such as istihada or chronic nosebleed), they must perform wudu for the time of each obligatory prayer, unlike al-Shafi'i who requires wudu for every prayer. The proof for the pregnancy ruling is the hadith that the Prophet forbade intercourse with pregnant captives until they deliver, indicating that hayd does not occur in pregnancy.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): The habitual menstrual blood that occurs at regular intervals.
استحاضة (istihada): Non-menstrual bleeding due to a vein rupture, which does not prevent prayer, fasting, or intercourse.
عادة (ada): The established pattern of a woman's hayd duration and intervals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 10 days
B) 20 days
C) 40 days
D) 60 days

Key Takeaway
Without a known habit, the Hanafi school sets a maximum of 10 days for hayd and 40 days for nifas; beyond is istihada.

intermediate
8 min
Pregnant Woman's Blood is Istihada
Ruling that pregnant women's blood is istihada with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a pregnant woman's blood is ruled as istihada in the Hanafi school, and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A pregnant woman's blood is istihada because the womb is closed, so it cannot be hayd.

This lesson addresses the ruling on blood seen during pregnancy. The Hanafi school classifies it as istihada (non-menstrual bleeding), not hayd. The reasoning is anatomical: the cervix closes during pregnancy, preventing menstrual blood from exiting. The proof is derived from a prophetic hadith that uses hayd as a sign of non-pregnancy. Understanding this ruling helps in properly applying purity rules for a pregnant woman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: 'Or what a pregnant woman sees is istihada.' This means any blood a pregnant woman observes is not hayd but istihada.
2
Understand the anatomical reason
The commentator explains that during pregnancy, the mouth of the womb (cervix) is closed, so blood cannot come from the uterus. Thus, the blood is from a burst vein, not from the womb, making it istihada.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The hadith: The Prophet prohibited intercourse with pregnant captives until they deliver, and with non-pregnant ones until they have one hayd (to establish non-pregnancy). This shows that hayd is a sign of an empty womb, so a pregnant woman cannot have hayd.
The dalil is a hadith, though its chain is not specified; it is sufficient for establishing the rule.
4
Apply the ruling
If a pregnant woman sees blood, it is istihada, meaning it does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations. She should treat it as a chronic hadath.
This is consistent with the general rules of istihada.

Book of Purification / Menstruation. 041. Or what a pregnant woman sees is istihada. Every month ten days, and what exceeds it is istihada, so her purity is twenty days. As for nifas, if the woman has no known habit regarding it, her nifas is forty days, and what exceeds it is istihada. [2] His saying: 'hayd from balaghat' in genitive as apposition to ten, and his saying: 'nifasuha' in genitive as apposition to forty. (Or what a pregnant woman sees is istihada): i.e., the blood that a pregnant woman sees is not hayd, but istihada. [1] His saying: ten days; because when she has no known habit to which her hayd matter can be referred, the maximum duration of hayd is considered; because her entry into hayd is certain, and the days are suitable for it, so one does not rule her exit from it by doubt, and what exceeds the maximum duration is necessarily istihada due to the days' unsuitability for hayd. Once. [2] His saying: twenty days; based on the fact that the majority of women menstruate once every month. [3] His saying: habit; he said in 'Jami' al-Rumuz': 'Know that the duration becomes a habit according to the two sides by two occurrences because it is derived from return, and according to him by one occurrence, and the fatwa is upon it as is well-known; for a young girl approaching puberty if she sees a single duration it becomes her habit, so if she sees two or more, then blood continues, she is referred to the repeated habit according to them, and to the last she saw according to him.' [4] His saying: by the genitive; and it can be by the nominative as predicate of an omitted subject, and ten is tanwin, and analogize to his saying: nifasuha. [0] His saying: or what a pregnant woman sees; i.e., the blood that a woman who has a fetus in her womb sees during her pregnancy days, this blood is not from the womb so as to be hayd due to the closure of the mouth of the womb during pregnancy, rather it is blood from a burst vein, so it is istihada. (1) End from 'Jami' al-Rumuz' (1:56). It does not prevent prayer, fasting, and intercourse, and one for whom no time of obligatory prayer passes except that he has a hadath from istihada, nosebleed, or the like, he performs wudu for the time of every obligatory prayer. His saying: 'wa ma naqsa' is mubtada, and his saying: 'fahuwa istihada' is its khabar. Then he explained the ruling of istihada, saying: (It does not prevent prayer, fasting, and intercourse, and one for whom no time of obligatory prayer passes except that he has a hadath i.e., the hadath with which he is afflicted, (from istihada, or nosebleed, or the like, performs wudu for the time of every obligatory prayer as a precaution against the saying of al-Shafi'i, for according to him he performs wudu for every prayer) and he prays supererogatory prayers following the obligatory. And it is indicated by what has been narrated in multiple reports that the Prophet, peace be upon him, forbade intercourse with pregnant captives until they give birth, and forbade intercourse with non-pregnant ones until they are free of a hayda, and that is only to know the purity of her womb from pregnancy, so he made hayd a sign of purity, so it is known that a pregnant woman does not have hayd.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn regarding hayd and istihada. It begins by stating that for a woman with no known habit, the maximum duration of hayd is ten days; any blood beyond that is istihada. The purity period between such hayds is twenty days, based on the norm that women menstruate once a month. For nifas (postpartum bleeding), the maximum is forty days if no habit is established; excess is istihada. The commentator then explains the grammatical construction of the matn: 'hayd min balaghat' is in the genitive as an apposition to 'ten', and 'nifasuha' is also genitive. For a pregnant woman, the blood she sees is not hayd but istihada, because the mouth of the womb is closed during pregnancy, so the blood is from a vein, not from the uterus. The commentator clarifies that the ten-day maximum is used because without a known habit, one relies on the maximum possible hayd duration, since we are certain she is in hayd initially and we cannot rule by doubt that she has left it. The habit formation is discussed: according to Abu Hanifa (with mufta bihi), one occurrence establishes a habit; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, two occurrences are needed. Finally, the commentator states the ruling of istihada: it does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations. For a person afflicted with a continual hadath (such as istihada or chronic nosebleed), they must perform wudu for the time of each obligatory prayer, unlike al-Shafi'i who requires wudu for every prayer. The proof for the pregnancy ruling is the hadith that the Prophet forbade intercourse with pregnant captives until they deliver, indicating that hayd does not occur in pregnancy.

Key Terms
حامل (hamil): A pregnant woman, i.e., a woman carrying a fetus.
استحاضة (istihada): Non-menstrual bleeding due to a vein rupture.
انسداد فم الرحم (insdad fam al-rahim): The closure of the mouth of the womb (cervix) during pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The blood is too little
B) The mouth of the womb is closed
C) The woman is too weak
D) The baby absorbs the blood

Key Takeaway
A pregnant woman's blood is istihada because the womb is closed and prophetic tradition confirms hayd only occurs in non-pregnant women.

Taharah — Purification

Habit Formation and Istihada Rulings
Explanation of habit formation and rulings of istihada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a woman's hayd habit is established according to the Hanafi school (intra-madhhab dispute) and apply the rulings of istihada regarding prayer, fasting, and wudu.

Core Idea
A habit is established by one occurrence (Abu Hanifa, mufta bihi); istihada requires wudu for each obligatory prayer time.

This lesson covers two topics: the formation of a woman's hayd habit (ada) and the practical rulings for istihada. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a habit is established by one or two occurrences, with the mufta bihi being Abu Hanifa's view (one occurrence). The lesson also explains that istihada does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations, and that a person with a chronic hadath (like istihada) performs wudu for the time of each obligatory prayer, not for every prayer like the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Define 'ada' (habit) for hayd
A habit is the woman's established pattern of hayd duration and interval. It is derived from the concept of 'return' (al-awd).
2
Understand the dispute on habit formation
Abu Hanifa holds that one occurrence establishes a habit; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require two. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view because it is practical for young girls.
The text quotes Jami' al-Rumuz: 'وعليه الفتوى كما هو المشهور' (the fatwa is upon it as is well-known).
3
Apply the habit to later bleeding
If a woman has a habit (e.g., 7 days) and then sees prolonged bleeding, she is referred to her habit: the days of habit are hayd, the rest istihada.
4
Identify the rulings of istihada
Istihada does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations. The bleeding is a chronic hadath that does not break wudu within the prayer time once wudu is performed.
5
Apply the wudu rule for chronic hadath
A person with continuous istihada (or similar) must perform fresh wudu for the time of each obligatory prayer, and can pray multiple obligatory and voluntary prayers with that wudu until the time expires.
This is a concession unlike the Shafi'i who require wudu for every prayer.

Taharah — Purification
