Purification of Visible Impurity by Removal of Essence
Explains ruling for visible impurity removal with any pure liquid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for purifying visible impurity and identify when a remaining trace is excused.

Core Idea
A visible impurity is removed when its essence is gone; a persistent trace that is hard to remove does not prevent purity.

This lesson explains the core Hanafi principle for removing visible impurities (najāsa mar'iyya). Unlike non-visible impurities which require washing and wringing, visible impurities are purified simply by removing their physical substance. The commentator clarifies that difficult-to-remove traces of color or smell are forgiven, but taste must be eliminated. We also examine the important dispute over whether only water or any pure liquid suffices for removal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Locate the statement that visible impurity is removed by removal of its essence (bi zawāl 'aynihi). Note that the text explicitly links this to the removal of the physical substance (jirm).
The ruling is a condition for prayer: the body, garment, and place must be free of visible impurity.
2
Examine the trace exception
Find where the author says 'even if a trace remains whose removal is difficult'. Understand that the school excuses traces of color or smell that require extra effort (e.g., soap) to eliminate. Taste, however, must be removed.
The principle of raf' al-haraj (removal of hardship) is the proof here.
3
Analyze the liquid requirement
The text states 'with water and with every pure, removing liquid'. Break down the conditions: the liquid must be pure (tāhir), flowing (mayyi'), and able to remove impurity (muzīl). Excluded: solid snow, impure liquids, thick liquids like oil.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The condition of 'removing' means the liquid can be wrung out or carries particles away.
4
Map the ikhtilāf
Note the disagreement: Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shāfi‘ī hold that only water purifies. Abū Yūsuf has a narration limiting other liquids to garments and places, not the body. Identify the practical consequence (thamara): a Hanafi can use vinegar; a Shāfi‘ī cannot.
The text does not give a clear tarjīh, but the muftā bihī in the Hanafi school is the permissive view due to raf' al-haraj.
5
Apply to practical cases
Review the examples: henna stain on hand, indigo dye on garment, child vomiting on breast, licking finger clean. See how the rule is applied consistently: the essence is removed even if trace remains.
These cases demonstrate that no specific number of washes is required after essence removal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة/ الأنجاس
بزوال عينه، وإن بقي أثر يَشقُ زوالُهُ بالماء وبكلِّ مائعٍ طاهرٍ مزيل بزوال عينه، وإن بقي أثرٌ يَشُقُ زوالُهُ بالماء) ، قوله : بالماء : متعلق بقوله : بزوال عينه، وبكل مائع طاهر مزيل وغير المرئي ما لا جرم له سواء كان له لون أم لا.
قوله : بزوال عينه ؛ أي ذاته ؛ لأن النجاسة حلت المحل، باعتبار العين، فتزول بزواله، وإن بقي أثر يشق : أي تكون إزالته محتاجة إلى حرج وكلفة، فإن الحرج مرفوع بالنصوص، فلو صبغ اليد بالحناء النجس فيطهر اليد بالغسل، وإن لم يزل اللون، صرح به في «الخلاصة»، وفي «الذخيرة». وفسروا المشقة بأن يحتاج إلى أمر آخر غير الماء كالأشنان والصابون. وفسروا الأثر بالرائحة واللون، فلو صبغ الثوبَ بالنيل النجس وغسله ثلاث مرات يطهر، وأما الطعم فلا بد من زواله ؛ لأنّ بقاء الطعم يدل على بقاء العين، كذا قال البرجندي، وفي الاكتفاء بذكر زوال العين إشارة إلى أنه لا يشترط في التطهير من المرئي عدد ولا غسل بعد زوال العين.
قوله : بالماء ؛ أي إذا كان طاهراً ، فإنَّ النجس لا يثبت وصف الطهارة، وهذا ظاهر ولظهوره لم يذكره ، فلو كان الماء مستعملاً كفى في إزالة النجاسات على المفتى به. قوله : وبكل مائع ؛ أي سائل من ماع الماء يميع : إذا سال على وجه الأرض ؛ أي يطهر بدن المصلي وثوبه ومكانه عن نجاسة مرئية بأن يزول عينها بكلّ سائل طاهر في نفسه، مزيل للنجاسة كالخل وماء الورد وغيرهما من المياه المقيدة. واحترز بالسائل عن الذي ليس كذلك كالثلج والبرد قبل الذوبان والسيلان، فإنّه لا تحصلُ الطهارة بهما ؛ لعدم حصول إزالة النجاسة بهما. واحترز بقيد الطاهر عن النجس كبول ما يؤكل لحمه على رأي أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ، فإنه منجس، فكيف يكون مطهراً ، ومنهم من حذف هذا القيد ؛ بناء على أنَّ السائل النجس يزيلُ النجاسة الأولى وتبقى نجاسة حاصلة به، وتظهر ثمرته في الحلفِ وغيره، مثلاً إذا كان الثوب نجساً بالدم فأزاله ببول مأكول اللحم، وحلف أن ثوبه ليست فيه نجاسة الدم لم يحنث.
كخل ونحوه وعما لم يُرَ أثره بغسله ثلاثاً، وعصره في كُلِّ مرةٍ إن أمكن كخل ونحوه وعما لم يُرَ أثره ) ، عطف على قوله : عن نَجَسٍ مَرْني، (بغسله ثلاثاً، وعصره في كُلِّ مرةٍ إن أمكن) واحترز بقيد المزيل عمّا ليس كذلك بأن لا ينعصر بالعصر، ولا تسيل أجزاء النجاسة به : كلبن وزيت ونحوهما مما فيه دسومة أو لزوجة، وهذا كله عند أبي يوسف وأبي حنيفة ، وعن أبي يوسف الله : إنّه يطهر الثوب والمكان بكلِّ مائع دون البدن ، فإنّه لا يطهر إلا بالماء. وعند محمد وزفر والشافعي لا تحصل الطهارة مطلقاً إلا بالماء دون المائعات. كذا في «الهداية» (١) ، ولكل من هذه الأقوال دلائل مبسوطة في حواشي «الهداية».
قوله ونحوه ؛ بالكسر ؛ أي ومثل الخلّ في إزالة النجاسة حتى الريق، وعلى هذا فرعوا طهارة ثدي المرأة إذا قاء عليه الولد ثمّ رضع حتى زال القيء، وكذا إذا لحس أصبعه من نجاسة حتى ذهب الأثر ، أو شرب خمراً ثم تردد ريقه في الفم مراراً طهرت أصبعه وفمه. كذا في «البحر الرائق».
Book of Purification / Impurities
By removal of its essence, even if a trace remains whose removal is difficult with water and with every pure, removing liquid by removal of its essence, even if a trace remains whose removal is difficult with water). His statement: 'with water' is attached to his statement: 'by removal of its essence, and with every pure, removing liquid. And the non-visible is that which has no body, whether it has color or not.
His statement: 'by removal of its essence' means its substance, because impurity settled in the place with respect to essence, so it is removed by its removal, even if a trace remains that is difficult: meaning its removal requires hardship and effort, for hardship is lifted by texts. So if the hand is dyed with impure henna, the hand is purified by washing, even if the color does not go away, as stated in 'al-Khulasa' and 'al-Dhakhira'. They interpreted difficulty as needing something other than water, like potash and soap. They interpreted trace as smell and color. So if a garment is dyed with impure indigo and washed three times, it becomes pure. As for taste, it must be removed because the persistence of taste indicates the persistence of essence, as al-Barjandi said. In limiting mention to removal of essence, there is indication that in purification from visible impurity, neither a specific number nor washing after removal of essence is required.
His statement: 'with water' means if it is pure, for impure water does not establish the attribute of purity. This is obvious, and due to its obviousness, he did not mention it. So if the water is used (musta'mal), it suffices for removal of impurities according to the fatwa. His statement: 'and with every liquid' means a fluid from the root 'mā'a' meaning 'to flow' when it flows on the ground; i.e., it purifies the body of the one praying, his garment, and his place from visible impurity by removing its essence with every fluid that is pure in itself, removing impurity, like vinegar, rosewater, and other restricted waters. He restricted to fluid to exclude what is not like that, such as snow and hail before melting and flowing, for purification is not achieved with them due to lack of removal of impurity. He restricted with the condition of pure to exclude impure, like the urine of an animal whose meat is eaten according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, for it is impure, so how can it be purifying? Some omitted this condition based on the idea that the impure fluid removes the first impurity and leaves a new impurity caused by it, and its practical effect appears in oaths and other matters. For example, if a garment is impure with blood and one removes it with urine of a halal animal, and he swears that his garment has no blood impurity, he does not break his oath.
Like vinegar and similar, and from what its trace is not seen, by washing it three times and wringing each time if possible, like vinegar and similar, and from what its trace is not seen), it is an apposition to his statement: from a visible impurity, (by washing it three times and wringing each time if possible). He restricted with the condition of removing to what is not like that, i.e., that cannot be wrung, and the particles of impurity do not flow with it, like milk, oil, and similar that have fat or viscosity. This all according to Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa. And from Abu Yusuf: he purifies the garment and place with every liquid except the body, for it is only purified with water. According to Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i, purification is absolutely not achieved except with water, not other liquids. As in 'al-Hidaya', and each of these positions has detailed proofs in the glosses of 'al-Hidaya'.
His statement: 'and similar' with kasra means like vinegar in removing impurity, even saliva. Based on this, they derived the purity of a woman's breast if a child vomits on it and then suckles until the vomit is removed, and similarly if he licks his finger from impurity until the trace goes away, or he drinks wine and then moves his saliva in his mouth repeatedly, his finger and mouth become pure. As in 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.'
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, explaining the rules for purifying visible impurities (najasa mar'iyya). The author opens by stating the core ruling: a visible impurity is removed by the removal of its essence (zawal al-'ayn). The matn says 'with water and with every pure, removing liquid.' The commentator explains that the impurity has settled in the place as an essence, so when that essence is gone, the impurity is gone. Even if a trace of color or smell remains that is difficult to remove, the purification is valid because hardship is removed by Islamic texts (raf' al-haraj). He gives examples: if henna (impure) stains the hand, washing purifies it even if the color persists. But taste must be removed because it indicates the presence of the essence. He notes that no specific number of washes is required after the essence disappears.

The commentator then explains that water used must be pure; impure water cannot purify. Used water (musta'mal) suffices according to the fatwa. The phrase 'with every pure, removing liquid' includes any liquid that is flowing, pure in itself, and can remove impurity, like vinegar and rosewater. He excludes solids like snow and hail before melting, and impure liquids like urine of edible meat animals according to the majority. However, some scholars say that even an impure liquid can remove the original impurity, but it leaves a new impurity, which matters in oaths.

The text then discusses the condition that the liquid must be 'removing' (muzil), i.e., able to be wrung or have particles flow. Thick liquids like milk and oil do not qualify. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. However, a narration from Abu Yusuf restricts this to garments and places, not the body, which only water can purify. Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i hold that nothing other than water purifies, whether for body, garments, or places.

Finally, the commentator gives practical applications: saliva can be used like vinegar; if a child vomits on the mother's breast and then suckles until the vomit is gone, the breast becomes pure. Similarly, licking an impure finger repeatedly until the trace is gone, or rinsing the mouth after drinking wine (though wine is impure in Hanafi school), purifies.

Key Terms
النجاسة المرئية (al-najāsa al-mar'iyya): Visible impurity that has physical substance (jirm) such that it can be seen, like blood or urine.
بزوال عينه (bi zawāl 'aynihi): By removal of the essence or substance of the impurity; the physical particles of impurity must be eliminated.
مائع مزيل (mayyi' muzīl): A liquid that is able to remove impurity by flowing and carrying away particles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum requirement for purifying a garment from visible impurity like blood?
A) Wash it three times with water.
B) Remove the essence of the impurity, even if color remains that is difficult to remove.
C) Use only pure water, no other liquids.
D) Wring the garment after each wash.

Key Takeaway
A visible impurity is purified by removing its physical essence with any pure, flowing liquid; difficult-to-remove traces of colour or smell are excused.

Purification of Non-Visible Impurity: Three Washes and Wringing in Hanafi Fiqh
Explanation of removing non-visible impurity by washing three times and wringing each time, and intra-Hanafi differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi method for purifying non-visible impurity, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on wringing, and apply the mufta bihi criterion of ghalabat al-zann.

Core Idea
Non-visible impurity is removed by washing until the washer is confident it is gone — not by a fixed number.

Cleaning a garment from non-visible impurities, like dried wine or urine traces, requires a specific procedure in the Hanafi school. The classical Zahir al-Riwayah states three washes with wringing each time, but later authorities adopted the principle of dominant probability (ghalabat al-zann). This lesson unpacks the source text and its intra-school differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Determine what is being purified (body, garment, place) and from what (non-visible impurity: no physical body after drying). The baseline ruling: purification is valid when the impurity is removed.
Non-visible impurity includes wine, diluted urine, etc., if no trace remains after drying.
2
Distinguish the Three Positions
State the three Hanafi views: Zahir al-Riwayah (three washes with wringing each time), Muhammad al-Shaybani (wringing only on last wash), Abu Yusuf (wringing not required at all).
These represent a spectrum from strict to lenient.
3
Understand the Proof for Wringing
The text argues wringing is necessary because it extracts the impurity absorbed in the fabric; mere washing does not remove absorbed particles. This is a causal reasoning (ta'lil).
The proof is based on the nature of impurity absorption, not a direct textual dalil.
4
Apply the Tarjih (Preference)
Identify the mufta bihi: ghalabat al-zann (dominant assumption of the washer) without specifying a number. This is the choice of al-Karkhi, al-Isbijabi, and the later authorities (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
The Zahir al-Riwayah is the apparent narration but not the operative fatwa.
5
Practice Real-Life Application
In daily life, if a garment has a non-visible impurity, wash until you are confident it is removed. You may wash three times and wring, but if you feel certain after two washes, that suffices.
Rely on your reasonable suspicion, not a fixed ritual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وعما لم ير ...الخ ؛ أي يطهر البدنُ والثوب والمكان عن نجاسة غير مرئية : وهي التي لا جرم لها ولا تحس بعد الجفاف، سواء كان له لون أم لا ، كذا في «خزانة الفتاوى»، بأن يغسل ذلك الثوبَ بالماء أو بالمائع الطاهر المزيل ثلاث مرات ويعصره كلّ مرّة إن أمكن عصر ذلك الشيء، فإن غسل ولم يعصر لا يطهر ؛ لأنّ المستخرج للنجاسةِ المتشرّبة في أجزاء الثوب هو العصر، ولا يكفي فيه مجرد الغسل، وهذا ظاهر الرواية. وعن محمد الله : إنه يكتفي به في المرة الأخيرة. وعن أبي يوسف : إنه ليس بشرط مطلقاً، كذا في شروح «المنية»، وذكر في «المنية» وغيرها : أنَّ المفتى به هو اعتبار غلبة ظنّ غاسل بزوال النجاسة من غير اشتراط العدد، وبه صرّح الكرخي والاسبيجابي ، وذكر في «السراج الوهاج»: إن اعتبار غلبة الظن مختار العراقيين، والتقدير بالثلاث مختارُ البخاريين ، والظاهر الأول إن لم يكن
His statement: 'and from what its trace is not seen...etc.' i.e., the body, garment, and place are purified from non-visible impurity: which has no body and is not perceptible after drying, whether it has color or not, as in 'Khizanat al-Fatawa'. That is, that garment is washed with water or with pure removing liquid three times, and wrung each time if wringing is possible. If it is washed without wringing, it does not become pure because the extractor of the impurity absorbed into the parts of the garment is wringing, and mere washing is not sufficient. This is apparent from the narration. From Muhammad: he suffices with wringing only on the last time. From Abu Yusuf: it is not a condition absolutely, as in the commentaries of 'al-Munya'. It is mentioned in 'al-Munya' and others that the fatwa is to consider the dominant assumption (ghalabat al-zann) of the one washing that the impurity has been removed, without stipulating a number. al-Karkhi and al-Isbijabi declared this. It is mentioned in 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj' that considering dominant assumption is the choice of the Iraqis, and estimation by three is the choice of the Bukharis, and the first is apparent if not...
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling for purifying non-visible impurities (najasa ghayr mar'iyya), such as traces of wine or urine that have no solid residue after drying. The commentator states that non-visible impurity is that which has no physical substance (jirm) and cannot be felt after it dries. The method of purification is to wash the item three times, wringing each time, using water or a pure removing liquid. Wringing is essential because it extracts the impurity that has been absorbed into the fabric; mere washing without wringing does not remove the absorbed particles. This is the apparent narration from the school (zahir al-riwaya).

There are differences within the Hanafi school: Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that wringing is required only on the last wash; Abu Yusuf holds it is not required at all; the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to al-Munya and others is to rely on the dominant probability (ghalabat al-zann) of the person washing that the impurity has been removed, without fixing a specific number of washes. This was the choice of the Iraqi scholars (al-Karkhi, al-Isbijabi). The Bukhari scholars (like al-Bazdawi) preferred the fixed number of three. The commentator leans toward the first view (dominant probability) as the apparent one, though the sentence is incomplete.

Key Terms
نجاسة غير مرئية (najāsa ghayr mar'iyya): Non-visible impurity: that which has no physical body (jirm) and is not perceptible after the item dries, e.g., traces of wine or diluted urine.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Dominant assumption or prevailing probability of the person washing that the impurity has been removed.
ظاهر الرواية (zāhir al-riwāya): The apparent or well-known narration within the Hanafi school, referring to the six books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) for purifying a garment from non-visible impurity according to later Hanafi authorities?
A) Wash exactly three times with wringing each time.
B) Wash three times, but wring only on the last time.
C) Wash until the person is confident that the impurity is removed, regardless of number.
D) Wringing is not required; just three pours of water suffice.

Key Takeaway
The purification of non-visible impurity in the Hanafi school is ultimately determined by the washer's dominant assumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the impurity is gone, not by a fixed number of washes.

Purification with Non-Water Liquids: Hanafi Rule, Dispute, and Applications
Inter-madhhab dispute on purification with non-water liquids, with conditions and derived cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification with non-water liquids, distinguish the positions of the three imams, and apply the ruling to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow purification with any pure, flowing liquid; Muhammad and al-Shafi'i require water absolutely.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a key point of difference within the school and with other madhhabs: can a garment or place be purified from impurity using a liquid other than water? The discussion reveals the conditions under which such purification is valid and the extent of disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The passage states that washing with vinegar and similar liquids removes impurity when the trace is not visible, by washing three times and wringing each time if possible.
This is the operative ruling according to Abu Hanifa and one narration from Abu Yusuf.
2
Understand the condition for validity
The liquid must be able to flow and carry away impurity particles (qayd al-muzil). Liquids with grease or viscosity, like milk and oil, are excluded because they do not dislodge the impurity.
This condition is highlighted by the phrase 'واحترز بقيد المزيل عمّا ليس كذلك'.
3
Map the intra-Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (except on the body) allow purification with any pure liquid. Muhammad and Zufar require water. Note Abu Yusuf's exception: the body is purified only with water.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf that involves the use of qiyas versus istihsan.
4
Identify the cross-madhhab position
Al-Shafi'i agrees with Muhammad and Zufar that water is absolutely necessary for purification.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: in the Hanafi school, vinegar, rose water, or even saliva can be used; other schools require water.
5
Apply to derived cases
The passage gives examples: a mother's breast purified by a child's suckling, a finger purified by licking, and a mouth purified by swallowing wine and swishing saliva. These show that the ruling extends to body parts and even impure substances like wine if they transform.
These furu' are based on the principle of izalat al-najasah (removal of filth) and the use of any pure liquid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كخل ونحوه وعما لم يُرَ أثره بغسله ثلاثاً، وعصره في كُلِّ مرة إن أمكن (كخل ونحوه) عطف على قوله : عن نَجَسٍ مَرْني، (بغسله ثلاثاً، وعصره في كُلِّ مرةٍ إن أمكن) واحترز بقيد المزيل عمّا ليس كذلك بأن لا ينعصر بالعصر، ولا تسيل أجزاء النجاسة به : كلبن وزيت ونحوهما مما فيه دسومة أو لزوجة، وهذا كله عند أبي يوسف وأبي حنيفة ، وعن أبي يوسف : إنّه يطهر الثوب والمكان بكلِّ مائع دون البدن ، فإنّه لا يطهر إلا بالماء. وعند محمد وزفر والشافعي لا تحصل الطهارة مطلقاً إلا بالماء دون المائعات.
قوله ونحوه ؛ بالكسر ؛ أي ومثل الخلّ في إزالة النجاسة حتى الريق، وعلى هذا فرعوا طهارة ثدي المرأة إذا قاء عليه الولد ثمّ رضع حتى زال القيء، وكذا إذا لحس أصبعه من نجاسة حتى ذهب الأثر ، أو شرب خمراً ثم تردد ريقه في الفم مراراً طهرت أصبعه وفمه. كذا في «البحر الرائق».
Like vinegar and similar, and for what has no visible trace, by washing it three times and wringing it each time if possible. (Like vinegar and similar) is connected to his statement 'from a visible impurity' (by washing it three times and wringing it each time if possible). And he restricted with the qualifier 'removing' what is not like that, i.e., that which cannot be wrung and whose impurity particles do not flow out, like milk, oil, and similar things that have grease or viscosity. This is all according to Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa. And from Abu Yusuf: that the garment and place are purified with every liquid except the body, for it is not purified except with water. And according to Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i, purification is absolutely not achieved except with water, not with other liquids.
His statement: 'and similar' with a kasrah, i.e., like vinegar in removing impurity, even saliva. And on this they derived the purity of a woman's breast if a child vomits on it and then suckles until the vomit is removed, and likewise if one licks his finger from impurity until the trace is gone, or drinks wine and then his saliva circulates in his mouth repeatedly, his finger and mouth become pure. So in 'al-Bahr al-Raiq'.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of using liquids other than water for ritual purification of garments, places, and the body from impurities (najasah). The author begins by stating the ruling: like vinegar and similar liquids can be used to wash away impurity, especially for invisible traces, by washing three times and wringing each time if possible. He then introduces a qualification: liquids that have grease or viscosity (like milk, oil) do not work because the impurity particles do not separate. The Hanafi school splits: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow purification with any pure liquid, but Abu Yusuf makes an exception for the body, requiring water. Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i require water absolutely. A marginal note clarifies the word 'and similar' includes saliva, and provides practical examples: if a child vomits on the mother's breast and suckles, or if one licks a finger with invisible impurity, the act of licking (which involves saliva) suffices as washing. The passage does not provide proofs but references detailed evidence in other works.

Key Terms
مائع (māiʿ): Any liquid other than water that is pure (tahir) and has the ability to remove impurity by flowing.
نجاسة مرئية (najāsa marʾiyya): An impurity that has a physical trace (color, smell, taste). Here it is contrasted with non-visible impurity.
قيد المزيل (qayd al-muzīl): The condition that the liquid must be capable of removing the impurity by flowing and carrying it away.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following liquids CANNOT be used for purification in the Hanafi school?
A) Vinegar
B) Rose water
C) Milk
D) Saliva

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, purification from najasah is achievable with any pure, flowing liquid that can physically remove the impurity, but this is contested by Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i who require water.

Washing Invisible Impurity: Three Washes and Wringing
Details the purification method for invisible impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing invisible impurity and identify the mufta bihi based on ghalabat al-zann.

Core Idea
Purification from invisible impurity relies on predominant assumption of removal, not a fixed number.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the method for purifying garments from non-visible impurities. While the apparent position (zahir al-riwayah) mandates three washes with wringing each time, the mufta bihi (operative opinion) is based on the washer's predominant assumption (ghalabat al-zann) of purity, without stipulating a number. Intra-Hanafi differences and practical considerations like exertion and solid objects are also addressed.

Guided Path
1
Define invisible impurity
Identify the type of impurity: it has no physical trace after drying, whether colored or not. This sets the context for the ruling.
From 'Kizanat al-Fatawa'.
2
State the zahir al-riwayah method
The apparent narration requires washing three times with water or pure liquid and wringing each time if possible. Wringing extracts absorbed impurity.
Mere washing without wringing does not purify.
3
Note intra-Hanafi differences
Muhammad requires wringing only in the last wash; Abu Yusuf does not require wringing at all. The school has internal variation.
These differences affect the stringency of the requirement.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is ghalabat al-zann (predominant assumption of purity) without a fixed number. This is the choice of Iraqi scholars. For someone with waswasa (excessive doubt), the three washes are recommended.
This reconciliation is from 'al-Nahr al-Faiq' and supported by 'al-Hidayah'.
5
Understand the dalil
The hadith of the one who wakes up from sleep (instructing three washes) provides support, but the ruling is not mechanically applied; it serves as a benchmark for achieving prevalent assumption.
The analogy to qibla direction is used: predominant assumption suffices when certainty is impossible.

قوله : وعما لم ير ...الخ ؛ أي يطهر البدنُ والثوب والمكان عن نجاسة غير مرئية : وهي التي لا جرم لها ولا تحس بعد الجفاف، سواء كان له لون أم لا ، كذا في «خزانة الفتاوى»، بأن يغسل ذلك الثوبَ بالماء أو بالمائع الطاهر المزيل ثلاث مرات ويعصره كلّ مرّة إن أمكن عصر ذلك الشيء، فإن غسل ولم يعصر لا يطهر ؛ لأنّ المستخرج للنجاسةِ المتشرّبة في أجزاء الثوب هو العصر، ولا يكفي فيه مجرد الغسل، وهذا ظاهر الرواية. وعن محمد : إنه يكتفي به في المرة الأخيرة. وعن أبي يوسف : إنه ليس بشرط مطلقاً، كذا في شروح «المنية»(۳)، وذكر في «المنية» وغيرها : أنَّ المفتى به هو اعتبار غلبة ظنّ غاسل بزوال النجاسة من غير اشتراط العدد، وبه صرّح الكرخي والاسبيجابي ، وذكر في «السراج الوهاج»: إن اعتبار غلبة الظن مختار العراقيين، والتقدير بالثلاث مختارُ البخاريين ، والظاهر الأول إن لم يكن موسوساً، وإن كان موسوساً فالثاني، وهذا توفيق حسن. كذا في «النهر الفائق»(۱) وذكر في «الهداية»: «إنَّ التكرار لا بدَّ منه للاستخراج، ولا يقطع بزواله، فاعتبر غالب الظن كما في أمر القبلة، وإنما قدروا بالثلاث ؛ لأنّ غالب الظن يحصل عنده ، فأقيم السبب الظاهر مقامه تيسيرا ، وتأييد ذلك بحديث المستيقظ من منامه). [١]قوله : يبالغ ؛ ولو لم يبالغ لرقّة الثوب مخافة ضياعه لا يطهـر علـى مـا في «الدرر»، واختار في «النهر» و «السراج» الطهارة للضرورة. [٢] قوله : في المرة الثالثة ؛ أشار به إلى أنه لا تشترط المبالغة في كل عصر، وظاهر «الخانية (٥) اشتراطها في كلّ مرّة، وعبارتها: «غسلُ الثوب ثلاثاً وعصره ه في كل مرة ، وقوته أكثر من ذلك، ولم يبالغ فيه صيانة للثوب لا يجوز). [۳] قوله : بقدر قوته ؛ أشار به إلى أنّ المعتبر هو قوة الغاسل وطاقته ؛ لأنّ كلّ أحد بوسعه لا غيره، فلو بالغ في العصر بحسب قوته فلم تبق قطرة، ثمَّ عصر بوسع آخر أقوى منه فقطر قطرة فهو طاهر بالنسبة إليه. كذا في شروح «المنية». [٤] قوله : وإلا ؛ أي وإن لم يكن عصره بأن يكون النجس شيئاً صلباً كالجلد والحصير ونحوهما. [0]قوله : ويترك ؛ أي يغسل مرّة ويترك إلى أن تتقاطر منه قطراته ، ثم يغسل
His statement: 'and for what has no visible trace...' i.e., the body, garment, and place are purified from non-visible impurity: which has no body and is not perceived after drying, whether it has color or not, as in 'Kizanat al-Fatawa', by washing that garment with water or with a pure removing liquid three times and wringing it each time if wringing of that thing is possible. If he washes without wringing, it does not become pure, because what extracts the impurity absorbed in the fibers of the garment is wringing, and mere washing is insufficient. This is the apparent narration. And from Muhammad: he is satisfied with it in the last time. And from Abu Yusuf: it is not a condition at all, as in the commentaries of 'al-Munya'. And it is mentioned in 'al-Munya' and others that the fatwa is based on considering the washer's prevailing assumption of the removal of impurity without stipulating a number. Al-Karkhi and al-Asbijabi explicitly stated this. And it is mentioned in 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj': that considering prevailing assumption is the choice of the Iraqis, and specifying three is the choice of the Bukharans. And the first is apparent if he is not a waswasi; if he is a waswasi, then the second. This is a good reconciliation. So in 'al-Nahr al-Faiq'. And it is mentioned in 'al-Hidayah': 'Repetition is necessary for extraction, and one cannot be certain of its removal, so prevailing assumption is considered, as in the matter of the qibla. They specified three because prevailing assumption is achieved thereby, so the apparent cause is substituted for it for ease. And this is supported by the hadith of the one who wakes up from sleep.' [1] His statement: 'he exerts himself'; if he does not exert himself due to the fineness of the garment for fear of ruining it, it does not become pure according to 'al-Durar', but 'al-Nahr' and 'al-Siraj' chose purity due to necessity. [2] His statement: 'in the third time' indicates that exertion is not required in every wringing. The apparent of 'al-Khaniyya' stipulates it in every time, and its expression: 'Washing the garment three times and wringing it each time, and his strength is more than that, and he did not exert himself to protect the garment – not permissible.' [3] His statement: 'according to his strength' indicates that what is considered is the washer's strength and capacity, because each person can only do what he is capable of. So if he exerts himself in wringing according to his strength until no drop remains, then another stronger person wrings it and a drop falls, it is pure relative to the first. So in the commentaries of 'al-Munya'. [4] His statement: 'otherwise' i.e., if wringing is not possible because the impure thing is solid like leather, mat, and similar. [0] His statement: 'and he leaves it' i.e., he washes once and leaves it until its drops drip, then washes again...
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on washing non-visible impurities from garments, bodies, and places. The author explains the definition: an impurity that has no physical trace after drying. The standard method is to wash three times with water or another pure liquid and wring after each wash if possible. Wringing is essential because it extracts the absorbed impurity. This is the 'zahir al-riwayah' position. Then intra-Hanafi differences are noted: Muhammad requires wringing only in the last wash, while Abu Yusuf does not require it at all. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is based on the washer's predominant assumption (ghalabat al-zann) of purity, without specifying a number. This is the choice of the Iraqi scholars (like al-Karkhi), while the Bukharans fix three washes. A reconciliation is offered: the first opinion applies unless the person is overly scrupulous (waswasi), in which case the fixed number is used. The reasoning from al-Hidayah is provided: repetition is needed for extraction, but certainty is impossible, so predominant assumption suffices, analogous to determining the qibla direction. The number three is an approximation because it usually yields that assumption. This is supported by the hadith of the one who wakes up from sleep (i.e., the instruction to wash three times after waking). Later marginal notes discuss the degree of exertion (mubalaghah) in wringing. If the washer does not exert fully due to fear of damaging a delicate garment, there is a difference: according to 'al-Durar' it does not become pure, but 'al-Nahr' and 'al-Siraj' rule purity due to necessity. The exertion is specifically required in the third wringing, not necessarily in each, but 'al-Khaniyya' requires it in every wringing. The strength of the washer is considered: if he exerts according to his ability, the garment becomes pure relative to him even if a stronger person could extract more. Finally, for solid objects like leather or mats that cannot be wrung, the method is to wash and leave to drip, then repeat.

Key Terms
العصر (al-ʿaṣr): Wringing or squeezing the garment after washing to extract the absorbed impurity.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-ẓann): Predominant assumption or strong probability that the impurity has been removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bihi regarding the number of washes for invisible impurity?
A) Exactly three washes are required.
B) One wash with wringing is enough.
C) It is based on the washer's prevailing assumption of purity.
D) The number depends on the type of impurity.

Key Takeaway
Purification from invisible impurity is based on predominant assumption of removal, not a fixed number of washes.

Purifying Solid Impure Objects by Washing and Dripping
Commentary on ruling for solid impurity purification with ghalabat al-zann

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi method for purifying an object contaminated with a solid impurity and explain the role of predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann) in determining the number of washes.

Core Idea
For solid impurities, repeated washing and dripping replace wringing; three washes suffice by predominant probability.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the ruling for purifying garments or mats contaminated with a solid impurity that cannot be wrung. The commentary builds on al-Hidayah's principle of relying on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann) and discusses the debated point of whether exertion in wringing is required every time or only in the third wash.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of impurity and method
Determine whether the impurity is solid (with physical mass) such that wringing is not possible. If so, the method is repeated washing and dripping until no more water drips.
Solid impurities include dry mud, stool, or blood that has dried.
2
Understand the role of predominant probability
The ruling is not based on certainty of removal (which is impossible) but on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann). Three washes are an estimate because they typically achieve this level of probability, analogous to determining qibla direction.
The number three is a guideline; if certainty or higher probability requires more, additional washes may be needed.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad required exertion in wringing in every wash. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf only required it in the third wash. The passage sides with the latter view, as indicated by 'in the third time'.
The disagreement is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Apply the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that exertion is required only in the third wash for absorbent objects. For solid impurities that cannot be wrung, the dripping itself suffices without wringing.
If the cloth is delicate and exertion would damage it, some authorities grant a concession of purification without full exertion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا يغسل ويترك إلى عدم القطران، ثم وثم هكذا بشرط أن يبالغ في العصر في المرة الثالثة بقدر قوته، وإلا يغسل ويترك إلى عدم القطران، ثم وثم هكذا موسوسا، وإن كان موسوسا فالثاني، وهذا توفيق حسن. كذا في «النهر الفائق» وذكر في «الهداية»: «إن التكرار لا بد منه للاستخراج، ولا يقطع بزواله، فاعتبر غالب الظن كما في أمر القبلة، وإنما قدروا بالثلاث؛ لأن غالب الظن يحصل عنده، فأقيم السبب الظاهر مقامه تيسيرا، وتأييد ذلك بحديث المستيقظ من منامه». [١] قوله: يبالغ؛ ولو لم يبالغ لرقة الثوب مخافة ضياعه لا يطهر على ما في «الدرر»، واختار في «النهر» و«السراج» الطهارة للضرورة. [٢] قوله: في المرة الثالثة؛ أشار به إلى أنه لا تشترط المبالغة في كل عصر، وظاهر «الخانية» اشتراطها في كل مرة، وعبارتها: «غسل الثوب ثلاثا وعصره في كل مرة، وقوته أكثر من ذلك، ولم يبالغ فيه صيانة للثوب لا يجوز». [٣] قوله: بقدر قوته؛ أشار به إلى أن المعتبر هو قوة الغاسل وطاقته؛ لأن كل أحد بوسعه لا غيره، فلو بالغ في العصر بحسب قوته فلم تبق قطرة، ثم عصر بوسع آخر أقوى منه فقطر قطرة فهو طاهر بالنسبة إليه. كذا في شروح «المنية». [٤] قوله: وإلا؛ أي وإن لم يكن عصره بأن يكون النجس شيئا صلبا كالجلد والحصير ونحوهما. [٠] قوله: ويترك؛ أي يغسل مرة ويترك إلى أن تتقاطر منه قطراته، ثم يغسل ويترك إلى أن ينتهي إلى عدم القاطر، ثم يغسل ويترك هكذا، فإن المقصود من العصر استخراج النجاسة بالتقاطر، فحيث لم يكن للعصر اعتبر نفس التقاطر.
Otherwise, wash it and leave it until it stops dripping, then again and again like this, on condition that he exerts effort in wringing in the third time according to his strength. 'Otherwise, wash and leave until it stops dripping, then again and again like this' for one who has waswasa, and if he has waswasa then the second time, and this is a good reconciliation. So in 'al-Nahr al-Fa'iq'. And it is mentioned in 'al-Hidayah': 'Repetition is necessary for extraction, and one cannot be certain of its removal, so predominant probability is considered as in the matter of the qibla, and they estimated it at three because predominant probability is attained thereby, so the apparent cause is made to stand in its place for facilitation, and this is supported by the hadith of the one who wakes from his sleep.' [1] His saying: 'he exerts effort'; if he does not exert effort due to the thinness of the cloth fearing its loss, it is not purified according to what is in 'al-Durar', and in 'al-Nahr' and 'al-Siraj' they chose purification out of necessity. [2] His saying: 'in the third time'; he indicated by it that exertion in every wringing is not required, and the apparent meaning of 'al-Khaniyyah' is that it is required in every time, and its wording: 'Washing the garment three times and wringing it each time, and its strength is more than that, and he did not exert effort to protect the garment, it is not permissible.' [3] His saying: 'according to his strength'; he indicated by it that the consideration is the strength and capacity of the washer; because everyone has his capability, not another's. So if he exerted effort in wringing according to his strength and no drop remained, then another stronger person wrung it and a drop came out, it is pure relative to him. So in the commentaries of 'al-Munyah'. [4] His saying: 'otherwise'; meaning if it is not possible to wring it, because the impurity is a solid thing like leather, mat, and the like. [0] His saying: 'and leave it'; meaning wash it once and leave it until its drops drip, then wash and leave until it stops dripping, then wash and leave like this. Indeed, the purpose of wringing is to extract the impurity by dripping, so where there is no wringing, the dripping itself is considered.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling for purifying a garment or similar object that has been contaminated with a solid impurity (like dry stool) that cannot be wrung out. The base text states that one should wash it and leave it to drip, repeating this process, with the condition that in the third wash one must wring it with full effort. The commentary clarifies that the repetition is necessary to remove the impurity, but since one cannot be certain of complete removal, the number three is set based on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann), analogous to determining the qibla direction. The hadith of the one who wakes from sleep is cited as support. The commentator then explains key terms: 'exert effort' means the washer must exert his full strength; if he refrains due to fear of damaging a delicate garment, there is a difference of opinion—some say it remains impure, while others rule it pure out of necessity. The phrase 'in the third time' indicates that exertion is only required in the final wringing, though some authorities require it in every wringing. 'According to his strength' means the standard is the washer's own capacity; if he wrings with all his might and no drops come out, the garment is pure for him, even if a stronger person could extract more. Finally, if the impurity is solid and cannot be wrung (e.g., leather, mat), then the process is simply repeated washing and dripping until no more drops appear, as the purpose of wringing is achieved by dripping.

Key Terms
غسل (ghusl): Washing the impure object with water to remove impurity; in this context, it refers to the act of washing a garment.
عصر (asr): Wringing or squeezing out water from the washed object to extract impurity-laden water.
تقاطر (taqattur): The process of water dripping from the object after washing; used as a substitute for wringing when the object cannot be wrung.
جرم (jurm): Solid substance of impurity; here it refers to impurities that have physical mass like stool or blood.
غالب الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Predominant probability; a level of certainty less than absolute (yaqin) that is sufficient in legal rulings where certainty is unattainable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in this passage, when must the washer exert full effort in wringing?
A) In every wash
B) In the second wash
C) In the third wash only
D) Not required at all

Key Takeaway
For solid impurities, purification is achieved by repeated washing and dripping until predominant probability of removal, typically three washes, without wringing.

Purifying a Khuff from Dry Solid Impurity by Rubbing
Ruling on purifying khuff from dry solid impurity by rubbing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purifying a khuff from dry solid impurity and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
A dry solid impurity on a khuff can be removed by rubbing on the ground — this is the mufta bihi position due to ease and a clear hadith.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the ruling on purifying a khuff (leather sock) when it becomes contaminated with a dry solid impurity. The author distinguishes between wet and dry, and between impurities with and without physical mass. Within the Hanafi school, there is a notable difference between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad. We will explore the evidence and the basis for the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being purified? A khuff (leather sock) or shoe. The impurity is dry and has substance (jurm). The ruling: rubbing on the ground suffices for purification according to Abu Yusuf.
The impurity type (dry, with mass) is crucial.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf allows rubbing; Imam Muhammad requires washing with water. Abu Hanifa also requires washing, but the text focuses on Muhammad's opposition.
The ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Examine the evidence
The hadith from Abu Dawud instructs wiping off filth from sandals and then praying. This is used to support rubbing for dry substances.
The hadith is ahad and has zanni thubut and dalala.
4
Understand the tarjih
The fatwa (mufta bihi) is on Abu Yusuf's view due to ease (taysir) and the general import of the hadith.
Tarjih authority: later Hanafi scholars prefer ease when supported by evidence.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if a khuff has dry blood or excrement, one rubs it on the ground until the dry substance is removed. Washing is not required.
This does not apply to wet impurities or liquid najasah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخُفُه عن ذي حِرْمٍ جَفٌ بالدِّلك بالأرض وجوزَهُ أبو يوسف له في رطبة إذا بالغ، وبه يُفْتَى. [١] قوله: وخفّه؛ ذكر في «الذخيرة» وغيرها: إنَّه إذا أصابت النجاسة خُفّاً أو نعلاً فإن لم يكن لها جرم كالبول والخمر فلا بد من الغَسل رطباً كان أو يابساً، وحكى عن الفضلي أبى بكر محمد بن الفضل أنه إذا أصابه بول أو خمر، ثم مشى على التراب أو الرمل حتى لزق به التراب وجف يكفي فيه المسح، وإن كان لها جرم كالعذرة والدم، فإن كانت رطبة لا تطهر إلا بالغسل. وعن أبي يوسف له إذا مسحه بالتراب ثم مسحه تطهر، وإن كانت يابسة يطهر بالمسح على الأرض، وفيه خلافُ محمد الله، فإنّه لا طهارة عنده إلا بالغسل عليه. والحديث حجّة عليه، وهو قوله: إذا جاء أحدكم المسجد فلينظر فإن رأى في نعليه قذراً أو أذى فليمسحه وليصل فيهما، أخرجه أبو داود، وهذا لفظه، وابن حبان والحاكم وإسحاق بن راهويه وأبو يعلى وغيرهم، وقد فصلت هذه المسألة مع تفاريعها ودلائلها في رسالتي: «غايةُ المقال فيما يتعلق بالنعال»، وتعليقاتي عليها المسماة بـ«ظفر الأنفال». [٢] قوله: وبه يفتى؛ لأنّ فيه تيسيراً، وإطلاق الحديث المذكور يؤيده.
And his khuff (leather sock) [is purified] from that which has substance, dry, by rubbing on the ground, and Abu Yusuf permitted it for him in wet [impurity] if he exerted effort, and by it fatwa is given. [1] His saying: 'and his khuff'; it is mentioned in 'al-Dhakhirah' and others: when impurity strikes a khuff or a shoe, if it has no substance like urine or wine, then washing is necessary whether wet or dry, and it is reported from al-Fadli, Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, that if urine or wine hits it, then he walks on dirt or sand until dirt sticks to it and it dries, wiping suffices for it; and if it has substance like excrement or blood, then if it is wet, it is not purified except by washing. And from Abu Yusuf too, if he wipes it with dirt then wipes it, it is purified, and if it is dry, it is purified by wiping on the ground, and there is disagreement of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), for there is no purification with him except by washing. And the hadith is evidence against him, which is his saying: 'When any of you comes to the mosque, let him look; if he sees filth or harm on his sandals, let him wipe it and pray in them.' Abu Dawud narrated it, and this is his wording, and Ibn Hibban, al-Hakim, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, Abu Ya'la, and others. And I have detailed this issue with its branches and proofs in my treatise 'Ghayat al-Maqal fima yata'allaqu bi al-Ni'al' and my annotations on it called 'Zafar al-Anfal'. [2] His saying: 'and by it fatwa is given'; because there is ease in it, and the generality of the mentioned hadith supports it.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage discusses the purification of a khuff (leather sock) or shoe that becomes impure. It distinguishes between impurities with substance (jurm) like excrement or blood, and those without substance like urine or wine. For impurities without substance, washing is generally required, but there is a narration from al-Fadli that if the khuff becomes dirty with urine or wine and then one walks on dirt until it dries, wiping suffices. For impurities with substance, if wet, only washing suffices; if dry, Abu Yusuf allows purification by rubbing on the ground, with or without dirt. Imam Muhammad requires washing in all cases. The author cites a hadith from Abu Dawud where the Prophet (ﷺ) instructed wiping off filth from sandals and then praying in them. This supports Abu Yusuf's position, and the fatwa is given accordingly due to ease and the general import of the hadith. The commentator notes that he has written a separate treatise on the matter.

Key Terms
خف (khuff): Leather sock or boot worn over the foot; subject to special purification rules.
جرم (jurm): Solid substance of impurity; here distinguishes between impurities with and without physical mass.
دلك (dalk): Rubbing or wiping; in this context, rubbing the khuff on the ground to remove dry impurity.
فتوى (fatwa): The relied-upon legal ruling; here, 'بها يفتى' indicates that Abu Yusuf's position is the operative one for practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), how is a khuff purified if it has dry solid impurity like dry blood?
A) Must be washed three times
B) Rubbed on the ground until impurity is gone
C) Wiped with a wet cloth
D) Only washing with soap is acceptable

Key Takeaway
For dry solid impurities on a khuff, rubbing on the ground suffices for purification according to the Hanafi fatwa.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Purification of Leather Socks from Dry Impurity (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on purification method for dry impurity on leather socks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling for purifying dry impurity with substance from leather socks, explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Yūsuf and Muhammad al-Shaybānī, and identify the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Rubbing dry impurity off leather socks suffices; Abu Yūsuf's view is the fatwa, supported by the hadith 'wipe and pray'.

When impurity with physical substance (like dry mud or blood) gets on your leather socks, the Hanafi school has a practical concession: you can simply rub it off on the ground. But not all Hanafi imams agree. This lesson unpacks the dispute between Abu Yūsuf and Muhammad, the evidence from hadith, and which view is preferred for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
Read the opening line: 'وخُفُه عن ذي حِرْم جَفٌ بالدِّلك بالأرض' – the ruling that a dry substance on a khuff is purified by rubbing on the ground. Note that this is attributed to Abu Yūsuf.
The phrase 'وبه يفتى' indicates this is the fatwa position.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Find where Imam Muhammad's opposing view is mentioned: 'وفيه خلاف محمد الله، فإنّه لا طهارة عنده إلا بالغسل'. He requires washing.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Examine the dalil (evidence)
The commentator cites the hadith: 'إذا جاء أحدكم المسجد فلينظر فإن رأى في نعليه قذراً أو أذى فليمسحه وليصل فيهما'. Understand how this supports Abu Yūsuf – the command to wipe implies rubbing is sufficient.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud and others; its transmission is zanni.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preference) reasoning
Note why the fatwa follows Abu Yūsuf: 'لأنّ فيه تيسيراً' – ease. The hadith's generality also supports.
Hanafi fatwa prioritizes ease when evidence is not definitive.
5
Derive the practical ruling
State the mufta bihi: for dry impurity with substance on khuff, rubbing on the ground (dalk) suffices. For wet, vigorous rubbing then washing.
This is the operative ruling for today.

And his sock from that which has substance, dry, becomes pure by rubbing on the ground, and Abu Yusuf permitted it for him in wet if vigorous, and upon it is the fatwa. Commentary: 'And his sock from that which has substance, dry, becomes pure by rubbing on the ground, and Abu Yusuf permitted it for him in wet' means in wet that has substance, if vigorous; and upon it is the fatwa, and it is left until it stops dripping, then washed and left like that, because the purpose of wringing is to extract the impurity by dripping, so where wringing is not possible, dripping itself is considered. (Footnote 1) say: 'and his sock'; it is mentioned in 'al-Dhakhira' and others: if impurity hits a sock or sandal, if it has no substance like urine or wine, washing is necessary whether wet or dry; and it is narrated from al-Fadli Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl that if urine or wine hits it, then he walks on dust or sand until dust sticks to it and it dries, wiping suffices. If it has substance like feces or blood, if wet it is not purified except by washing. And from Abu Yusuf: if he wipes it with dust then wipes it, it becomes pure; if dry, it becomes pure by wiping on the ground. And there is a difference from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): purification for him is only by washing. The hadith is evidence against him: 'When one of you comes to the mosque, let him look; if he sees filth or harm on his sandals, let him wipe it and pray in them' – reported by Abu Dawud (this is his wording), Ibn Hibban, al-Hakim, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, Abu Ya'la, and others. I have detailed this issue with its branches and proofs in my treatise 'Ghayat al-Maqal fima Yata'allaq bi al-Ni'al' and my comments on it called 'Zufr al-Anfal'. (2) say: 'and upon it is the fatwa' because there is ease in it, and the generality of the mentioned hadith supports it. And from that which has no substance, only by washing, and from semen by washing it or rubbing it dry. Commentary: 'And from that which has no substance, only by washing' means the sock is purified from what has no substance like urine by washing only. 'And from semen by washing it' (whether wet or dry) 'or rubbing it dry'. If you say: its generality includes non-substance as well, why did they not allow wiping for it? I say: that which has no substance is excluded by indication of its rationale, because dust is a purifier for it, i.e., removing impurity; we know certainly that the sandal or sock, if it absorbs urine or wine, wiping on the ground does not remove it nor extract it from the parts of the leather. (1) say: 'and from semen'; this conjunction is not free from difficulty, because its apparent meaning implies it is conjoined to 'from that which has substance' or 'from that which has no substance'. It is objected: the ruling is not specific to the sock and similar; the ruling applies to garment and body. So the correct view is that it is conjoined to 'from visible impurity', and if this issue were placed before the issue of the sock it would be better. (2) say: 'or rubbing it dry' because of the hadith of 'Aisha: 'I used to wash janaba – i.e., semen – from the garment of the Prophet (peace be upon him),' reported by Muslim and the authors of the Sunan; and she also said: 'I used to rub semen from his garment,' reported by Muslim and others; and in a narration of al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi: 'I used to wash semen from the garment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when it was wet, and rub it when it was dry.' There are many reports and traditions on this topic, whose elaboration is in 'al-Sa'aya'. (3) say: 'its dry'; whether it is semen of a man or semen of a woman, and on the first, whether it is...
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's rulings on purifying leather socks (khuff) and sandals from different types of impurity (najasah), and also the ruling on semen (mani). It begins by stating the rule: if a dry impurity with physical substance (jurm) is on the khuff, it becomes pure by rubbing (dalk) on the ground. This ruling is attributed to Abu Yusuf, with the condition that the impurity is wet and one rubs vigorously; this is the fatwa position. The commentator explains that after rubbing, one lets it drip until no more liquid comes, then washes it and leaves it as is, because the purpose of wringing is extraction by dripping – when wringing is not possible, dripping alone suffices. The text then cites from 'al-Dhakhira' a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad requires washing for any impurity with substance, while Abu Yusuf allows rubbing for dry and wet (with vigorous rubbing). The hadith reported by Abu Dawud (wipe and pray) is used as evidence supporting Abu Yusuf's view. The fatwa is given with Abu Yusuf's position due to ease and the generality of the hadith. Next, the passage addresses impurities without substance (like urine): only washing purifies; rubbing is ineffective because the liquid is absorbed. Finally, semen is treated: it can be purified by washing (whether wet or dry) or by rubbing if dry, based on the hadith of 'Aisha. The commentator clarifies that this ruling is not restricted to the khuff but applies generally to garments and skin. The conjunction in the matn is problematic, but the correct reading is that the ruling for semen is mentioned as a separate category. Overall, the lesson presents a practical, lenient approach to purification of leather items and semen, relying on authentic hadith.

Key Terms
خف (khuff): A leather sock or foot covering worn inside shoes, especially for wiping in wudu
جرم (jurm): Physical substance or solid matter of an impurity (e.g., blood, feces)
دلك (dalk): Rubbing or scouring the affected area, here specifically on the ground to remove impurity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in this passage, how is dry najasah with substance (like dry blood) on a leather sock purified?
A) Only by washing with water
B) By rubbing on the ground vigorously until it stops dripping, then washing
C) By wiping with a damp cloth
D) By leaving it until it becomes pure on its own

Key Takeaway
For dry impurity with substance on leather socks, rubbing on the ground purifies according to the Hanafi fatwa, despite Imam Muhammad's stricter view.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Ruling on Semen: Washing or Rubbing Dry
Ruling on purification of semen: washing or rubbing dry, based on hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that semen can be purified by washing or, if dry, by rubbing, and explain the hadith evidence from Aisha.

Core Idea
Semen is pure in the sense that dry rubbing suffices; Aisha's practice of rubbing dry semen is authority.

The passage extends the discussion of purification to semen (mani), a common concern. The matn states that semen can be purified by washing or, if dry, by rubbing. This lesson examines the scope of this ruling and the evidence from the hadith of Aisha, and clarifies that the ruling applies universally to garments and skin, not only to leather socks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for semen
Read: 'وعن المني بغسله أو فرك يابسه' – semen is purified by washing (whether wet or dry) or by rubbing if dry.
The term 'farak' (rubbing) is used specifically for dry semen.
2
Understand the universal application
The commentary notes that this ruling is not restricted to the khuff; it applies to any garment or body part.
The conjunction in the matn is clarified: the ruling is general, not a continuation of the khuff discussion.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Aisha's hadith: she used to wash wet semen and rub dry semen from the Prophet's garment. This is reported by Muslim, Abu Dawud, etc.
The hadith is sahih and directly supports the permissibility of farak for dry semen.
4
Note the scope: male and female semen
The text states that the ruling applies to semen of both man and woman; it is the same.
Some earlier differences existed, but the fatwa treats both equally.

And his sock from that which has substance, dry, becomes pure by rubbing on the ground, and Abu Yusuf permitted it for him in wet if vigorous, and upon it is the fatwa. Commentary: 'And his sock from that which has substance, dry, becomes pure by rubbing on the ground, and Abu Yusuf permitted it for him in wet' means in wet that has substance, if vigorous; and upon it is the fatwa, and it is left until it stops dripping, then washed and left like that, because the purpose of wringing is to extract the impurity by dripping, so where wringing is not possible, dripping itself is considered. (Footnote 1) say: 'and his sock'; it is mentioned in 'al-Dhakhira' and others: if impurity hits a sock or sandal, if it has no substance like urine or wine, washing is necessary whether wet or dry; and it is narrated from al-Fadli Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl that if urine or wine hits it, then he walks on dust or sand until dust sticks to it and it dries, wiping suffices. If it has substance like feces or blood, if wet it is not purified except by washing. And from Abu Yusuf: if he wipes it with dust then wipes it, it becomes pure; if dry, it becomes pure by wiping on the ground. And there is a difference from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): purification for him is only by washing. The hadith is evidence against him: 'When one of you comes to the mosque, let him look; if he sees filth or harm on his sandals, let him wipe it and pray in them' – reported by Abu Dawud (this is his wording), Ibn Hibban, al-Hakim, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, Abu Ya'la, and others. I have detailed this issue with its branches and proofs in my treatise 'Ghayat al-Maqal fima Yata'allaq bi al-Ni'al' and my comments on it called 'Zufr al-Anfal'. (2) say: 'and upon it is the fatwa' because there is ease in it, and the generality of the mentioned hadith supports it. And from that which has no substance, only by washing, and from semen by washing it or rubbing it dry. Commentary: 'And from that which has no substance, only by washing' means the sock is purified from what has no substance like urine by washing only. 'And from semen by washing it' (whether wet or dry) 'or rubbing it dry'. If you say: its generality includes non-substance as well, why did they not allow wiping for it? I say: that which has no substance is excluded by indication of its rationale, because dust is a purifier for it, i.e., removing impurity; we know certainly that the sandal or sock, if it absorbs urine or wine, wiping on the ground does not remove it nor extract it from the parts of the leather. (1) say: 'and from semen'; this conjunction is not free from difficulty, because its apparent meaning implies it is conjoined to 'from that which has substance' or 'from that which has no substance'. It is objected: the ruling is not specific to the sock and similar; the ruling applies to garment and body. So the correct view is that it is conjoined to 'from visible impurity', and if this issue were placed before the issue of the sock it would be better. (2) say: 'or rubbing it dry' because of the hadith of 'Aisha: 'I used to wash janaba – i.e., semen – from the garment of the Prophet (peace be upon him),' reported by Muslim and the authors of the Sunan; and she also said: 'I used to rub semen from his garment,' reported by Muslim and others; and in a narration of al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi: 'I used to wash semen from the garment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when it was wet, and rub it when it was dry.' There are many reports and traditions on this topic, whose elaboration is in 'al-Sa'aya'. (3) say: 'its dry'; whether it is semen of a man or semen of a woman, and on the first, whether it is...
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's rulings on purifying leather socks (khuff) and sandals from different types of impurity (najasah), and also the ruling on semen (mani). It begins by stating the rule: if a dry impurity with physical substance (jurm) is on the khuff, it becomes pure by rubbing (dalk) on the ground. This ruling is attributed to Abu Yusuf, with the condition that the impurity is wet and one rubs vigorously; this is the fatwa position. The commentator explains that after rubbing, one lets it drip until no more liquid comes, then washes it and leaves it as is, because the purpose of wringing is extraction by dripping – when wringing is not possible, dripping alone suffices. The text then cites from 'al-Dhakhira' a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad requires washing for any impurity with substance, while Abu Yusuf allows rubbing for dry and wet (with vigorous rubbing). The hadith reported by Abu Dawud (wipe and pray) is used as evidence supporting Abu Yusuf's view. The fatwa is given with Abu Yusuf's position due to ease and the generality of the hadith. Next, the passage addresses impurities without substance (like urine): only washing purifies; rubbing is ineffective because the liquid is absorbed. Finally, semen is treated: it can be purified by washing (whether wet or dry) or by rubbing if dry, based on the hadith of 'Aisha. The commentator clarifies that this ruling is not restricted to the khuff but applies generally to garments and skin. The conjunction in the matn is problematic, but the correct reading is that the ruling for semen is mentioned as a separate category. Overall, the lesson presents a practical, lenient approach to purification of leather items and semen, relying on authentic hadith.

Key Terms
فرك (farak): Rubbing dry impurity (like dry semen) to remove it from fabric or skin
مني (mani): Semen

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The garment must be washed three times
B) It can be rubbed off (farak)
C) It is not necessary to remove it
D) Only water purifies it

Key Takeaway
Semen can be purified by washing or, if dry, by rubbing; this is based on the authentic practice of Aisha.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Purifying Khuff, Mani, and Smooth Surfaces: Hanafi Rulings on Special Cases
Commentary on rulings for purifying khuff, mani, and smooth surfaces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for purifying khuff from urine, mani by washing or scraping, and smooth surfaces by wiping, and explain the evidence and the intra-Hanafi difference on body purification.

Core Idea
Mani has a special light ruling: washing or scraping dry suffices; unlike other impurities that require washing for absorbent surfaces.

In this passage, the commentator expands on three purification cases: leather socks (khuff) from light impurities, semen (mani) from garments and body, and smooth items like swords. Each has a distinct ruling based on the nature of the impurity and the surface. We will explore the evidence, the grammatical debate, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the body can be purified by scraping dry semen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three purification cases and their basic rulings.
The passage covers three cases: (1) leather sock (khuff) from impurities without hurmah (like urine) is purified only by washing (ghasl). (2) Semen (mani) is purified by washing (wet or dry) or by scraping (farak) when dry. (3) Smooth surfaces (sword, mirror, glass) are purified by wiping (mash) on a pure surface to remove the impurity. Note the special status of mani: it is considered najis but with a light ruling.
Mani's ruling is an exception to the usual rule requiring full washing for najasah.
2
Examine the primary evidence: the hadith of Aisha.
The main proof for purifying mani by scraping is the hadith where Aisha (ra) said: 'I used to rub semen from the garment of the Prophet (peace be upon him).' This is a fi'li (action-based) Sunnah. The commentator cites multiple narrations: 'I would wash it when wet and rub it when dry.'
The hadith is narrated by Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, and others.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah: how the hadith indicates purification by scraping.
The key word 'afruku' (I rub/scrape) shows that the Prophet (pbuh) did not order washing when dry; he allowed scraping. In Hanafi usul, the Prophet's action (fi'l) indicates permissibility (ibahah) and, when done consistently, indicates sunnah. Since the impurity is removed by scraping, the action demonstrates that dry mani is purified without water.
The hadith is zanni in both thubut and dalala, so the ruling is not fard but sunnah muakkadah.
4
Explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on purifying the body by scraping.
In the nawadir (rare transmissions), Hasan ibn Ziyad reports from Abu Hanifa that the body is not purified by scraping because body heat attracts the impurity, so water is required. The commentator responds that the small amount retained is excused by necessity (taysir), just as the impurity trapped in the pores of a garment is excused.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) view is that scraping suffices for the body as well, as per al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
5
Apply the ruling to smooth surfaces and understand the principle.
Swords and similar items (mirror, nail, bone, glass) have no pores (la masama lahu), so they do not absorb impurity. Therefore, wiping (mash) on a pure surface that removes the trace of impurity suffices, regardless of whether the impurity is wet or dry. This is a general principle for non-absorbent surfaces.
This ruling is based on the fact that impurity only sits on the surface and can be mechanically removed.

وعما لا حِرْمَ له بالغَسْلِ فقط ) : أي يَطْهُرُ الخُفَّ عَمَّا لا حِرْمَ له كالبول بالغَسْل فقط.
( وعن المني بغسله ( سواء كان رطباً أو يابساً، (أو فرك" يابسه) فإن قلت : إطلاقه شامل لغير ذي جرم أيضاً فما بالهم لم يجوزوا فيه إلا الغسل. قلت : الذي لا جرم له خرج بإشارة تعليله ، فإنّ التراب لها طهور ؛ أي مزيل للنجاسة، فإنّا نعلم يقيناً أنّ النعل والخف إذا شرب البول أو الخمر لا يزيله المسحُ بالأرض، ولا يخرجه من أجزاء الجلد.

وعن المني؛ هذا العطفُ لا يخلو عن إشكال، فإنّ ظاهره يقتضي أنه معطوف على قوله : «عن ذي جرم»، أو على قوله : «عما لا جرم له». ويرد عليه : إنه لا اختصاص لما ذكره في الخف ونحوه، فالحكم يعم الثوب والبدن، فالصحيح أنه عطف على قوله : «عن نجس مرئي»، ولو قدم هذه المسألة على مسألة الخف لكان أولى.

أو فرك يابسه؛ لحديث عائشة : « كنت أغسل الجنابة - أي المني - من ثوب النبي، أخرجه مسلم وأصحاب السنن ، وقالت أيضاً : «كنت أفرك المني من ثوبه)، أخرجه مسلم وغيره وفي رواية الدار قطني والبيهقي: «كنت أغسلُ المني من ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا كان رطباً، وأفركه إذا كان یا بسا، وفي الباب أخبار وآثار كثيرة، موضع بسطها هو «السعاية».

يابسه؛ سواء كان مَنِي الرجل أو مَنِي المرأة، وعلى الأول سواء كان رقيقاً لمرض أو غليظاً، وكذا الحكم في مني جميع الحيوانات، وهذا هو المعتمد على ما في «الدر المختار» وحواشيه، وفيه اختلاف كثير.

بأن بال.... الخ؛ فإن لم يكن رأس الذكر طاهراً فمنيه لا يطهر بالفرك؛ لاختلاطه بنجس غيره، وطهارة المني بالفركِ إنّما ثبتت بالآثار على خلاف القياس، فلا يتعدّى إلى غيره. فإن قلت : المني يكون مخلوطاً بالمذي، فإنّ الرجل يمذي ثمّ يمني، ومن المعلوم أنّ المذي لا يطهر بالفرك، فكيف يطهر المني المخلوط به؟ قلت : لما حكم الشارعُ بطهارة محلّ المني بالفرك، عُلِمَ أَنه عفى عما يختلط به من المذي للضرورة، ولا كذلك غيره من النجاسات.

لا يطهر البدن بالفرك؛ بناءً على أنَّ حرارة البدن جاذبة له، فلا يزيله عنه إلا الماء.

Book of Purification / Impurities. And from what has no ritual significance by washing only, and concerning semen by washing it or rubbing (scraping) it when dry. And from what has no ritual significance by washing only: meaning the leather sock is purified from what has no ritual significance like urine by washing only. And concerning semen by washing it (whether wet or dry) or rubbing it when dry. If you say: Its generality includes also that which has no substance, so why did they not allow for it except washing? I say: That which has no substance is excluded by the indication of its rationale, for indeed earth is purifying for it, meaning it removes impurity, for we know with certainty that the sandal and leather sock when they absorb urine or wine, wiping with earth does not remove it, nor does it bring it out from the parts of the skin. And concerning semen; this conjunction is not free from difficulty, for its apparent meaning requires that it is conjoined to his statement: 'from what has substance' or to his statement: 'from what has no substance'. And it is objected: That what he mentioned is not specific to the leather sock and the like, rather the ruling applies to clothes and the body. So the correct view is that it is conjoined to his statement: 'from visible impurity'. And if he had placed this issue before the issue of the leather sock it would have been better. Or rubbing it when dry; due to the hadith of Aisha: 'I used to wash janabah - meaning semen - from the garment of the Prophet', narrated by Muslim and the authors of the Sunan, and she also said: 'I used to rub (scrape) semen from his garment', narrated by Muslim and others, and in the narration of al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi: 'I used to wash semen from the garment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when it was wet, and I would rub it off when it was dry.' And in this chapter there are many reports and traditions, the place for their detail is in 'al-Si'aya'. Its dryness; whether it is the semen of a man or the semen of a woman, and in the first case whether it is thin due to illness or thick, and likewise the ruling for the semen of all animals. This is the relied-upon view according to what is in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' and its glosses, and there is much difference (of opinion) in it. That he urinated... etc.; if the tip of the male organ is not pure, then his semen is not purified by rubbing due to its mixing with another impurity. And the purification of semen by rubbing is only established by reports contrary to analogy, so it is not extended to other things. If you say: Semen is mixed with pre-seminal fluid, for a man emits pre-seminal fluid then semen, and it is known that pre-seminal fluid is not purified by rubbing, so how can semen mixed with it be purified? I say: When the Lawgiver ruled the place of semen to be pure by rubbing, it was known that He overlooked what is mixed with it from pre-seminal fluid due to necessity, and not so for other impurities. It (the body) is not purified by rubbing; based on the fact that the heat of the body attracts it, so nothing removes it except water. The response: That amount is excused for ease and necessity, just as the amount drawn into the pores of the garment is excused. And the sword; meaning the sword and the like from smooth items that have no pores: such as a mirror, a nail, bone, glass, and others, are purified by wiping them on a pure thing by which the trace of impurity is removed, whether it is wet or dry, without need for washing; because these things do not absorb impurity, and what is on their surface is removed by wiping.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) expanding on the rulings from the base text (al-Wiqayah) on purification of leather socks (khuff), semen (mani), and smooth surfaces. The author explains that a khuff is purified from impurities like urine by washing only, not by rubbing with earth, because the impurity is absorbed into the leather. For semen (mani), the ruling is special: it can be purified by washing (whether wet or dry) or by rubbing/scraping when dry. This is proven by authentic hadith from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her). The commentator addresses a potential objection about the generality of the ruling, noting that items without substance (like polished surfaces) are excluded because the rationale (ta'lil) using earth shows that only absorbent items are covered. The passage then discusses a grammatical issue with the conjunction, concluding that the ruling on semen applies generally to clothes and body, not just to khuff. An intra-Hanafi difference is mentioned: in the narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, the body is not purified by rubbing dry semen because body heat attracts it, requiring water. The commentator responds that the small amount is excused due to necessity. Finally, the ruling on swords and similar smooth items (mirror, nail, bone, glass) is given: they are purified by wiping the impurity off onto a pure surface, without washing, because they do not absorb impurities. This is based on the principle that when the impurity is only on the surface, removal by wiping suffices. Throughout, the author stays within the Hanafi madhhab, citing relied-upon sources like al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

Key Terms
الخُفّ (al-khuff): Leather sock worn over the foot, used in rules of wiping (mash) and purification.
المني (al-maniy): Semen, which is considered ritually impure (najis) in the Hanafi school but with a light ruling allowing purification by scraping when dry.
الفرك (al-fark): Rubbing or scraping dry impurity off a surface to remove it; sufficient for purifying dry mani.
حرمة (hurmah): Ritual significance or inviolability; impurities with hurmah are those that affect the validity of prayer (like urine, blood).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school, how is a leather sock (khuff) purified from urine?
A) By wiping with earth
B) By washing with water only
C) By scraping when dry
D) By wiping on a pure surface

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides distinct purification methods based on the type of impurity (semen is light) and the absorbency of the surface (porous vs. smooth), with special evidence overriding general rules.

Taharah — Purification
