Purification of Smooth Surfaces (Sword, Mirror, Bone, Glass) by Wiping
Commentary on the matn ruling for purifying smooth objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the method of purifying smooth, non-porous surfaces from najasah in the Hanafi school and identify which surfaces qualify.

Core Idea
A smooth surface without pores is purified by wiping the impurity away—no water needed.

The Hanafi school recognizes that not all surfaces absorb najasah. This lesson covers the ruling for items like swords, mirrors, and glass: their lack of pores means they are purified simply by wiping off the impurity. The commentator explains the basis for this leniency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the surface type
Determine if the surface is smooth and non-porous (e.g., polished metal, glass, bone). If porous, wiping does not suffice.
The text specifies 'smooth polished items without pores'.
2
State the ruling
For such surfaces, the impurity (wet or dry) is removed by wiping on a clean substance; the surface becomes pure.
No washing required.
3
Examine the condition
The surface must not absorb the impurity; the trace must be removable by wiping.
If the impurity is absorbed, wiping does not work.
4
Apply to examples
Sword, mirror, fingernail, bone, glass – all are purified by wiping.
The commentator lists these explicitly.
5
Understand the rationale
Because these materials do not absorb, the impurity is only on the surface and easily removed.
This is based on the nature of the material, not a divine command.

٥٥٥
والبساط يجري الماء عليه ليلة، والأرض والآجُرُّ المفروش باليُبس، وذهاب الأثر للصلاة لا للتيمم والبساط يجري الماء عليه ليلة (۱۸) ، والأرض والآجر (۲۸) المفروش باليُبس" ،وذهاب الأثر للصلاة لا للتّيمم) : أي يجوزُ الصَّلاة عليهما
[١]قوله : يجري الماء عليه ليلة؛ قال الشارحُ الهروي : هذا موافق لما في «الظهيرية» و«الخلاصة» (۳)، و«خزانه المفتين»، وفي «الكافي»: يوماً وليلة، والظاهر أنه المراد بليلة؛ أي مع يومها، وفي العبارة إشارة إلى أنه لا بد من الجريان.
[٢] قوله : والآجر؛ بمد الهمزة، وضم الجيم، وتشديد الراء المهملة، بالفارسية : خشت، وإنّما قيده بالمفروش؛ أي على الأرض؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن مفروشاً، بل مثبتاً ينقل ويحوّل لا يكون في حكم الأرض، فلا يطهر بالجفاف؛ ولذا قيدوا طهارة الشجرِ ونحوه مما يتصل بالأرض اتصال قرار بكونه قائماً في الأرض، فإنّ المقطوع ليس له حكمه.
[٣] قوله : باليبس؛ لما يدلّ عليه حديث أبي داود وغيره عن ابن عمر : «كانت الكلاب على عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم تبول وتقبلُ وتدبر في المسجد، فلم يكونوا يرشون شيئاً : «زكاةُ الأرض يبسها (٦) (4) ، ويؤيده قول أبي جعفر محمد بن علي من أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، وفي الباب أخبار وآثار أخر أيضاً.
ذلك
And the sword and similar items [are purified] by wiping. [Semen] thin due to illness or thick, likewise the ruling for semen of all animals. This is the relied-upon position as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses, and there is much difference. (1) [1] His saying: 'if he urinated...' etc. If the head of the penis is not pure, his semen is not purified by rubbing because it is mixed with other impurity. Purification of semen by rubbing is established by traditions contrary to analogy, so it does not extend to other things. If you say: semen is mixed with madhi, for a man emits madhi then semen, and it is known that madhi is not purified by rubbing, so how is the mixed semen purified? I say: When the Lawgiver ruled that the place of semen is purified by rubbing, it is known that He pardoned what is mixed with it of madhi due to necessity, and not so for other impurities. [2] His saying: 'the body is not purified by rubbing' is based on the fact that the body's heat attracts it, so nothing removes it except water. Its answer: That amount is pardoned for ease and necessity, just as was pardoned the amount drawn into the pores of the garment. [3] His saying: 'and the sword' means the sword and similar polished items without pores, like a mirror, nail, bone, glass, etc., are purified by wiping it on a pure thing that removes the trace of impurity, whether it is wet or dry, without need for washing, because these things do not absorb impurity, and what is on their surface is removed by wiping. (1) (al-Durr al-Mukhtar 1:313). The book of Purification / Impurities. 555 And the carpet: water is run over it for a night, and the earth and baked bricks laid down [are purified] by dryness, and removal of the trace [suffices] for prayer, not for tayammum. And the carpet: water is run over it for a night, and the earth and baked bricks laid down by dryness, and removal of the trace for prayer, not for tayammum; i.e., it is permissible to pray on them. [1] His saying: 'water is run over it for a night.' The commentator al-Harawi said: This agrees with what is in al-Zahiriyya, al-Khulasa, and Khizanat al-Muftiyin, and in al-Kafi: a day and a night, and apparently what is meant by 'a night' is along with its day. And in the phrase there is an indication that running is necessary. [2] His saying: 'and baked bricks' (al-ajur) with a long hamza, damma on jim, and shadda on ra, in Persian: khisht. He restricted it by 'laid down', i.e., on the ground, because if it is not laid down but fixed and movable, it is not in the ruling of earth, so it is not purified by drying. Hence they restricted purification of trees and the like attached to the earth with a fixed attachment to being standing in the earth, for the cut one does not have its ruling. [3] His saying: 'by dryness' due to what the hadith of Abu Dawud and others from Ibn Umar indicates: 'Dogs used to urinate and come and go in the mosque during the time of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and they would not sprinkle anything [on it]: The zakah (purification) of earth is its dryness.' And it is supported by the saying of Abu Ja'far Muhammad ibn Ali reported by Ibn Abi Shayba, and there are other reports and traces in this chapter.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on al-Wiqaya, expanding on rulings for purifying different objects from najasah. It begins with the purification of a sword and similar smooth, non-porous items (like mirrors, bone, glass) by wiping them on a clean surface until the trace of impurity is gone—no washing required because they do not absorb. The same applies to any impurity, wet or dry. An intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: in the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, the body is not purified by rubbing semen; the commentator explains this is because body heat attracts it, but the majority view pardons a small amount for ease. The passage then addresses purification of a large carpet by running water over it for a night (or a day and night) until the water flows through, ensuring the impurity is removed. For earth and baked bricks that are fixed as flooring, drying (yubs) suffices for purification, based on the hadith 'the zakah of earth is its dryness' and supporting athar. However, this removal of trace by drying is valid for prayer but not for tayammum, which requires dust for purification. The commentator clarifies that the baked bricks must be laid down (fixed to the ground) to gain the ruling of earth; if movable, they are like other vessels that require washing. Trees attached to the earth are also purified by drying only when standing, not when cut.

Key Terms
فَرْك (fark): Rubbing a dry impurity off a surface to remove its trace.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for purifying a sword by wiping in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because it is metal
B) Because it has no pores and does not absorb impurity
C) Because the Prophet wiped his sword
D) Because all impurities are removed by rubbing

Key Takeaway
Smooth, non-porous surfaces are purified simply by wiping off the najasah because they do not absorb impurity.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
7 min
The Ruling on Purifying the Body from Semen: Rubbing vs. Washing
Intra-Hanafi commentary on purification of semen from the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on purifying semen from the body and identify the relied-upon view regarding the pardon of a small trace.

Core Idea
Semen on the body requires washing, but a small remaining trace is pardoned for necessity.

The purification of semen from the body involves a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute. While the general rule is that washing is needed, the majority opinion pardons a trace that cannot be removed due to body heat, drawing an analogy with the pardon of impurity drawn into clothing pores. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and initial ruling
Semen on the body: initial ruling is that washing is required; rubbing (fark) does not suffice.
This is based on the difference between body and garments.
2
Understand the objection
The narration from Hasan (from Abu Hanifa) holds that the body is not purified by rubbing at all because body heat attracts the semen.
This is a stricter view.
3
Analyze the majority response
The majority hold that any minute trace left after rubbing is pardoned for ease and necessity.
Analogy: amount drawn into clothing pores is also pardoned.
4
Identify the relied-upon position
The commentator sides with the majority: trace is pardoned, and one may pray without washing.
This is the mufta bihi view.
5
Practical takeaway
If you rub off semen from your body and only a faint trace remains due to body heat, your body is pure and prayer is valid.
The trace must be minimal; if substantial, washing is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسيف ونحوه بالمسح
رقيقاً لمرض أو غليظاً، وكذا الحكم في مني جميع الحيوانات، وهذا هو المعتمد على ما في «الدر المختار» وحواشيه، وفيه اختلاف كثير.
(1)
[1 ] قوله : بأن بال.... الخ؛ فإن لم يكن رأس الذكر طاهراً فمنيه لا يطهر بالفرك؛ لاختلاطه بنجس غيره، وطهارة المني بالفرك إنّما ثبتت بالآثار على خلاف القياس، فلا يتعدّى إلى غيره. فإن قلت : المني يكون مخلوطاً بالمذي، فإنّ الرجل يمذي ثمّ يمني، ومن المعلوم أنّ المذي لا يطهر بالفرك، فكيف يطهر المني المخلوط به؟ قلت : لما حكم الشارعُ بطهارة محلّ المني بالفرك، عُلِمَ أَنه عفى عما يختلط به من المذي للضرورة، ولا كذلك غيره من النجاسات. [۲] قوله : لا يطهر البدن بالفرك؛ بناءً على أنَّ حرارة البدن جاذبة له، فلا يزيله عنه إلا الماء. وجوابه: إن ذلك القدر معفو عنه تيسيراً وضرورة، كما عفـي عـن القـدر المنجذب في مسام الثوب. [٣]قوله : والسيف؛ أي يطهر السيف ونحوه من صقيل لا مسام له : كمرآة، وظفرة، وعظم وزجاج ، وغيرها، بمسحه على شيء طاهر يزولُ به أثر النجاسة، سواءً كانت رطبة أو يابسة، من غير حاجة إلى الغَسل؛ لأنّ هذه الأشياء لا تنشرب النجاسة، وما على ظاهرها يزول بالمسح.
(۱) (الدر المختار (۱ : ۳۱۳).

Purification of Carpets by Running Water and Earth by Drying
Commentary on the matn rulings for purifying carpets and earth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Hanafi method for purifying a large carpet and explain the ruling that earth is purified by drying, with its conditions.

Core Idea
A large carpet is purified by running water through it; earth and fixed baked bricks are purified by drying.

The Hanafi school provides distinct methods for purifying different surfaces. This lesson examines the rulings for carpets (requiring running water) and earth or fixed flooring (purified by drying alone). The proof is the hadith 'the zakah of earth is its dryness,' supported by practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items
The text discusses three items: a large carpet (bisaat), earth (ard), and baked bricks laid down (ajurr mafroosh).
Each has a specific method.
2
For the carpet: method and condition
Water must be run over the carpet for at least a night (interpreted as a day and night). The water must flow through it.
Running is necessary; standing water does not suffice.
3
For earth and fixed bricks: drying suffices
Drying (yubs) is sufficient for purification, based on the hadith and practice.
The bricks must be fixed to the ground; if movable, they are like vessels and require washing.
4
Note the limitation on the trace
The removal of the trace of impurity (dhahab al-athar) validates prayer on that surface, but does not suffice for tayammum.
Tayammum requires the earth to be essentially pure (inherently pure), not just ritually pure by drying.
5
Apply the proof
The hadith of Ibn Umar is cited: dogs urinated in the mosque, and they did not sprinkle water; the Prophet said 'the zakah of earth is its dryness.'
This is a clear evidence for drying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the carpet: water is run over it for a night, and the earth and baked bricks laid down by dryness, and removal of the trace for prayer, not for tayammum; i.e., it is permissible to pray on them. [1] His saying: 'water is run over it for a night.' The commentator al-Harawi said: This agrees with what is in al-Zahiriyya, al-Khulasa, and Khizanat al-Muftiyin, and in al-Kafi: a day and a night, and apparently what is meant by 'a night' is along with its day. And in the phrase there is an indication that running is necessary. [2] His saying: 'and baked bricks' (al-ajur) with a long hamza, damma on jim, and shadda on ra, in Persian: khisht. He restricted it by 'laid down', i.e., on the ground, because if it is not laid down but fixed and movable, it is not in the ruling of earth, so it is not purified by drying. Hence they restricted purification of trees and the like attached to the earth with a fixed attachment to being standing in the earth, for the cut one does not have its ruling. [3] His saying: 'by dryness' due to what the hadith of Abu Dawud and others from Ibn Umar indicates: 'Dogs used to urinate and come and go in the mosque during the time of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and they would not sprinkle anything [on it]: The zakah (purification) of earth is its dryness.' And it is supported by the saying of Abu Ja'far Muhammad ibn Ali reported by Ibn Abi Shayba, and there are other reports and traces in this chapter.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on al-Wiqaya, expanding on rulings for purifying different objects from najasah. It begins with the purification of a sword and similar smooth, non-porous items (like mirrors, bone, glass) by wiping them on a clean surface until the trace of impurity is gone—no washing required because they do not absorb. The same applies to any impurity, wet or dry. An intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: in the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, the body is not purified by rubbing semen; the commentator explains this is because body heat attracts it, but the majority view pardons a small amount for ease. The passage then addresses purification of a large carpet by running water over it for a night (or a day and night) until the water flows through, ensuring the impurity is removed. For earth and baked bricks that are fixed as flooring, drying (yubs) suffices for purification, based on the hadith 'the zakah of earth is its dryness' and supporting athar. However, this removal of trace by drying is valid for prayer but not for tayammum, which requires dust for purification. The commentator clarifies that the baked bricks must be laid down (fixed to the ground) to gain the ruling of earth; if movable, they are like other vessels that require washing. Trees attached to the earth are also purified by drying only when standing, not when cut.

Key Terms
يُبْس (yubs): Drying, the method by which the earth and fixed flooring become pure from najasah.
آجُرّ (ajurr): Baked bricks used as flooring; when fixed to the ground, they take the ruling of earth for purification.
جَرَيَان (jaryaan): Flow of water; the condition for purifying a carpet – water must run through it, not just stand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the evidence that earth is purified by drying in the Hanafi school?
A) Quranic verse: 'And We send down pure water'
B) Hadith: 'The zakah of earth is its dryness'
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Analogy to carpets

Key Takeaway
The earth and fixed flooring become pure from najasah by drying, based on the prophetic practice and the hadith that the purification of earth is its dryness.

Purification of Standing Plants by Drying: The Hanafi Distinction
Ruling on purification of impure land plants by drying and prohibition of tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purification of impure plants by drying and distinguish between connected and cut plants.

Core Idea
Connected plants become pure by drying; cut plants must be washed — the concession is limited to the earth and its attachments.

In matters of taharah, the Hanafi school recognizes that the earth itself can be purified by drying. But what about plants that grow from the earth? This lesson examines the ruling on standing trees and grass that become contaminated and then dry, and the crucial distinction between plants still rooted and those that have been cut. The passage also clarifies that tayammum cannot be performed with such plants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being purified: plants rooted in the earth (shajar, kala', khas) that become impure and then dry. The ruling is that they become pure by drying.
2
Distinguish between connected and cut plants
Note the key distinction: if the plant is still connected to the earth, it purifies by drying. If cut, it does not; it must be washed.
This distinction is based on the principle of connection to the earth.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The purification of earth by drying is a concession (khilaf al-qiyas) established by the Quran. This concession is not extended to other things except what is directly connected to the earth.
The Arabic phrase 'على خلاف قياس' indicates exceptional ruling.
4
Note the tayammum prohibition
Tayammum is not permissible with these plants because the Quran specifies 'sa'id' (pure earth) for tayammum. Ahad hadith cannot override the Quranic text.
The word 'صعيدًا طيبًا' is restricted to earth.
5
Evaluate the authority
The ruling is presented as the chosen (mukhtar) view in al-Hidayah, reflecting the authority of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الخص وشجر وكلاً قائم في الأرض لو تنجس، ثم جف طهر، هو المختار، وما قطع منهما يغسله لا غير
[قوله : ولا يجوز التيمم ؛ لأنَّ طهارة ما يتيمم به ثبتت بنص الكتاب، فلا تتأدى بما ثبت بأخبار الآحاد. كذا في «الهداية»
[قوله : وما قطع منهما ؛ أي الشجر والكلأ إذا قطع من الأرض وانفصل لا يطهر إلا بالغسل لا غير؛ لأنّ طهارة الأرض باليبس ثبتت على خلاف قياس ، فلا تتعدى إلى غيره، وغير ما هو متصل به
And likewise the mat (of reeds) and tree and grass, all standing in the ground, if they become impure, then become dry, they become pure—that is the chosen view. And what is cut from them, one washes it, nothing else. [He said: And it is not permissible to perform tayammum with them, because the purity of that with which tayammum is performed is established by the text of the Book, so it cannot be achieved by what is established by ahad reports. Thus in al-Hidayah. [He said: And what is cut from them, i.e., the tree and grass when cut from the ground and separated, are not purified except by washing, nothing else, because the purity of earth by drying is established contrary to analogy, so it does not extend to other than it and other than what is connected to it.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about the purification of land plants (such as reeds, trees, and grass) that become impure while still rooted in the earth. If such plants are contaminated with najasah and then become dry (i.e., the liquid impurity dries up and its trace disappears), they become pure according to the chosen (mukhtar) view. However, if those plants are cut from the ground and then become dry, they do not become pure by mere drying; they must be washed. The reason is that the purity of earth by drying is an exceptional ruling (khilaf al-qiyas) established only for the earth itself and what is directly connected to it. Cutting them severs that connection, so the exception does not apply. Tayammum is also not permissible with these plants because the permissibility of tayammum is proven by the Quran (which uses the word sa'id), and that cannot be overridden by ahad hadith reports. The authority weight of this ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah as it references al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution using pure earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl.
شجر (shajar): Tree or plant that is rooted and growing in the ground.
كلأ (kala'): Grass or herbage, also rooted in the ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a cut branch from a tree that became impure and then dried?
A) It becomes pure by drying.
B) It must be washed to become pure.
C) It is permanently impure.
D) It can be used for tayammum.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that connected plants become pure by drying, but cut plants require washing; tayammum is not permissible with plants.

Heavy Impurity and the Excused Dirham
Defining heavy impurity and the dirham amount excused, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define heavy impurity (najas ghaliz) according to the Hanafi school, state the excused amount (dirham), and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over classification criteria.

Core Idea
A dirham of heavy impurity is excused in prayer; but what defines 'heavy' is disputed between Abu Hanifa and his students.

The Hanafi school recognizes a concession (afw) for small amounts of heavy impurity in acts of worship, specifically up to the size of a dirham (a small coin). But what exactly counts as 'heavy' impurity is not entirely settled within the school. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the ruling and lists the classic examples, while later commentary reveals a subtle difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn on how to define the heavy category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that a dirham (weight/volume) of heavy impurity is excused (عفو) in prayer. Note that the subject is 'the amount of a dirham' and its predicate is 'excused'.
The dirham is a measure of weight or volume, not area.
2
Recognize Examples of Heavy Impurity
The passage lists urine (human and from non-edible animals), poured blood (مسفوح), wine, and feces. Study the commentary's exceptions: non-poured blood is not heavy, and the martyr's blood is excused.
Wine is unanimously heavy; other liquid intoxicants are debated.
3
Distinguish the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
From the toolbox, note that Abu Hanifa defines heavy impurity as that for which there is a text declaring impurity with no conflicting text. The Sahibayn define it as that about which there is disagreement on impurity. This affects items like donkey urine.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's definition due to 'umum al-balwa (general difficulty).
4
Apply the Ruling
In practice, if a worshipper finds a dirham-sized stain of human urine on clothing, the prayer is valid. If the stain is larger, it must be removed. For light impurity (najas khafif), up to a quarter of the garment is excused.
The classification of an impurity as heavy or light changes the excused amount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[قوله : من نجس بفتح الجيم، غليظ على وزن فعيل، صفة لنجس، وفي نسخة: غلظ بضم اللام على وزن كرم.
[قوله : كبول؛ الظاهر أنّ المراد به بولُ الآدمي وإن كان صبياً رضيعاً، فإنّ بوله نجس أيضاً، وكذا كل ما خرج من الآدمي موجباً لوضوء أو غُسل، ويحتمل أن يراد بولُ كل ما لا يؤكل لحمه ويستثنى منه بولُ الخفاش، فإنّه طاهر، وكذا خرؤه. كذا في «الدر المختار».
[قوله : ودم؛ أي مسفوح من أي حيوان كان لما مر أن غير المسفوح ليس بنجس، ويستثنى منه دم الشهيدِ ما دامَ على بدنه، كما حققه في «البحر».
[قوله : وخمر؛ كونه نجساً مغلظاً اتفاقي، وكذا نجاسة باقي المسكرات المائعة، واختلف في كونها مغلظة أو مخفّفة.

And the amount of a dirham of heavy impurity, like urine, blood, wine, and feces. [He said: 'And the amount of a dirham' is the subject, its predicate is his saying 'excused', and his saying 'less than a quarter of a garment' is conjoined to the subject. [He said: 'Of heavy impurity' with a fatha on the jim, 'heavy' on the pattern of fa'il, an adjective for impurity, and in a copy: 'ghuluth' with a damma on the lam on the pattern of karam. [He said: 'Like urine': apparently what is meant is human urine, even if it is a nursing infant, for his urine is also impure, and likewise everything that exits from a human that necessitates wudu or ghusl. It is possible that it means the urine of every animal whose meat is not eaten, and bat urine is excepted, for it is pure, and likewise its feces. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [He said: 'And blood': i.e., poured blood from any animal, as previously mentioned that non-poured blood is not impure, and the blood of a martyr on his body is excepted as long as it remains on him, as verified in al-Bahr. [He said: 'And wine': its being a heavy impurity is agreed upon, and likewise the impurity of other liquid intoxicants. But there is disagreement on whether they are heavy or light. As for non-liquid intoxicants like opium and saffron...
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage defines the amount of heavy impurity that is excused (ma'fu 'anhu) in prayer: the size of a dirham (approximately 3.36 grams or a small coin). Heavy impurity (najas ghaliz) includes urine (human or from animals not eaten), poured blood, wine, and feces. The commentary explains each item: human urine is heavy even from a nursing infant; blood must be poured (safuh) from any animal, except the martyr's blood on his body; wine is unanimously heavy, but liquid intoxicants besides wine are disputed as heavy or light. The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf appears in the later discussion (not fully quoted here) regarding the classification of the impurity of donkey urine and other matters. The authority weight of this ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah as it appears in the matn.

Key Terms
نجس غليظ (najas ghaliz): Heavy impurity: impurity that is more severe and has a smaller excused amount (dirham).
عفو ('afw): Excused or pardoned: the impurity is present but does not invalidate the prayer due to its small quantity.
درهم (dirham): A unit of weight or coin; used as a measure for the excused amount of heavy impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, up to a dirham of heavy impurity is excused in prayer, but the classification of heavy vs. light depends on the school's internal definition, with the view of the Sahibayn being preferred for fatwa.

Excused Amounts of Impurities in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining excused amounts and explaining intra-madhhab classification differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the excused amounts for thick and light impurities and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on classification of impurities.

Core Idea
Thick impurity up to a dirham, light up to a quarter of a garment are excused, but even these are makruh to leave.

The Hanafi school recognizes that small amounts of impurity do not invalidate prayer. However, the amounts differ based on the type of impurity and there is an internal disagreement on how to classify certain impurities as thick or light. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the commentary of al-Wiqayah.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish thick vs light impurities
Identify the two categories: thick (najas ghaliẓ) like human urine, blood, wine, dung; light (najas khafif) like urine of edible animals, bird droppings.
The classification affects the excused amount.
2
Learn the excused amounts
Thick impurity is excused up to the size of a dirham (approx. 2.5 cm diameter). Light impurity is excused up to less than a quarter of the garment.
These amounts are from ijma and hadith of istinja.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa classifies an impurity as thick if there is a clear text of impurity with no opposing text. The Sahibayn classify as light if there is any scholarly disagreement (e.g., Malik's view). Example: dung is thick for Abu Hanifa, light for Sahibayn.
The mufta bihi follows Sahibayn on disputed items.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
Given a scenario (e.g., a drop of horse urine on the sleeve), determine if it is excused based on its category and amount.
Remember that even the excused amount makes prayer makruh tahrimi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة/ الأنجاس
وقَدْرُ الدرهم من نَجَس غليظ كبول، ودم وخمر، وخرء
[1]
فقال : ( وقَدْرُ الدرهم من نَجَس ) غليظ كبول، ودم" وخمر، وخرء وجد فيه نص معارض فهو مخفّف ؛ كبول ما يؤكل لحمه، وعندهما: ما اختلف في نجاسته فهو مخفف ، وما لم يكن كذلك فهو غير مخفف.
فالروث مغلظ عنده ؛ لأنه ورد نص بتسميته ركساً بالكسر؛ أي نجساً، ولم يعارضه نص آخر، وعندهما : مخفف ؛ لوقوع الاختلاف فيه ؛ لقول مالك ه بطهارته لعموم البلوى، وليطلب تفصيل هذا المقام من المطولات.
[١] قوله : وقدر الدرهم ؛ هو مبتدأ ، خبره قوله : «عفو»، وقوله : ما دون ربع ثوب عطف على المبتدأ.
[٢] قوله : من نجس ، بفتح الجيم، غليظ على وزن فعيل ، صفة لنجس، وفي نسخة : غلظ بضم اللام على وزن كرم. [0]قوله : وخمر ؛ كونه نجساً مغلظاً اتفاقي، وكذا نجاسة باقي المسكرات المائعة ، واختلف في كونها مغلظة أو مخفّفة، وأمّا المسكرات الغير المائعة(۳) كالأفيون والزعفران

11 قوله : وبول حمار ؛ إنّما أفرده بالذكر دفعاً لإيهام أن بوله مشكولاً كلعابه، وأما الهرة: بكسر الهاء، وتشديد الراء المهملة ، بالفارسية : كربه، والفأرة بالفارسية : موش ، فإنّما نص على بولهما دفعاً لقول من قال من الفقهاء بطهارة بولهما. The Book of Purification / Impurities. And the measure of a dirham of thick impurity such as urine, blood, wine, and dung. [1] He said: (And the measure of a dirham of impurity) thick such as urine, blood, wine, and dung - if there is an opposing text it is light; like urine of what is edible meat, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): whatever is disputed in its impurity is light, and what is not so is not light. Dung is thick according to him (Abu Hanifa) because a text came naming it 'riks' meaning impure, and no other text opposed it, while according to them it is light due to the existence of disagreement about it because of Malik's statement of its purity due to general affliction, and the detail of this topic is sought from lengthy works. [1] His statement: 'the measure of a dirham' is the subject, its predicate is his statement: 'excused', and his statement: 'less than a quarter of a garment' is a conjunction to the subject. [2] His statement: 'from impurity' with fatha on jim, 'ghaliẓ' on the pattern of fa‘īl, adjective for impurity, and in a copy: 'ghuluẓ' with damma on lam on the pattern of 'karm'. [3] His statement: 'like urine' - apparently what is meant is human urine even if a nursing infant, for his urine is also impure, and likewise everything that exits from a human necessitating wudu or ghusl, and it is possible that it means urine of every animal whose meat is not eaten, and the urine of the bat is excluded for it is pure, and similarly its dung. This is in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [4] His statement: 'and blood' - i.e., flowing blood from any animal because what is not flowing is not impure, and the blood of the martyr while on his body is excluded, as verified in al-Bahr. [0] His statement: 'and wine' - its being a thick impurity is agreed upon, and likewise the impurity of other liquid intoxicants, but there is disagreement whether they are thick or light. As for non-liquid intoxicants like opium and saffron. [PAGE_556] Chicken, and donkey urine, and cat urine, and mouse urine, and dung, and elephant dung, and less than a quarter of the garment of what is light like horse urine and what eats meat and the dung of birds not eaten is excused even if it increases. 'No' is apparent as verified in Radd al-Muhtar. [11] His statement: 'and donkey urine' - he mentioned it separately to remove the illusion that its urine is like its saliva. As for cat (hirrah) with kasr ha and tashdid ra, in Persian: gurbah, and mouse (fa’rah) in Persian: mush, he mentioned their urine to refute those jurists who say their urine is pure. [2] His statement: 'and dung and elephant dung' - al-rawth (with fatha on ra) is from horse, mule, and donkey like human waste, al-khutha (with kasra then sukun) for cow and elephant, al-ba‘rah for sheep and camel, as al-khar’ for birds, al-najw for dog, and al-‘adhirat and al-gha’it for human. This is in Radd al-Muhtar. In summary, the waste of every animal is impure except birds. [3] His statement: 'excused' - i.e., regarding the validity of prayer with it, not regarding sin, for keeping the excused amount and performing prayer with it is makruh tahrimi, so washing it is obligatory. As for less than that, it is makruh tanzihi, so washing it is sunnah. This is verified by the commentators of al-Kanz and al-Munyah. The reasoning: the evidence of ijma and athar testifies that a certain amount of impurity is excused, and there is no obligation to remove every impurity even if small. So we set that as less than a quarter for light impurities because the quarter has the ruling of the whole in many rulings, and as a dirham for thick impurities, taken from the hadiths of istinja' with stones, for it is known that the stone is desiccating and wiping, not a remover, and the Lawgiver excused it, and the place of stool is approximately a dirham in size.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on al-Wiqayah dealing with the amounts of impurity that are excused (ma'fu 'anhu) in prayer. The author begins by stating that a thick impurity (najas ghaliẓ) such as human urine, blood, wine, and solid dung is excused up to the size of a dirham. Then he discusses a key intra-Hanafi difference: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, if there is a textual proof declaring something impure and no opposing text, it is classified as thick impurity. However, if there is disagreement about its impurity (even from other schools), the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it light impurity, because the existence of difference lessens the certainty. For example, animal dung (rawth): Abu Hanifa considers it thick because a hadith calls it 'riks' (impure) with no counter-text. The Sahibayn consider it light because Imam Malik holds it pure due to general affliction (umum al-balwa). The passage then defines key terms: 'najas ghaliẓ' means thick impurity, and 'najas khafif' means light impurity. It clarifies that 'the measure of a dirham' is the subject excused, and 'less than a quarter of a garment' is an additional subject. The commentary explains that the urine mentioned refers to human urine, including that of infants, and also the urine of animals whose meat is not eaten, except bat which is pure. Blood means flowing blood; non-flowing blood (like liver) is not impure. Martyr's blood on his body is excused. Wine is unanimously thick, but other liquid intoxicants have disputed status; non-liquid intoxicants like opium are different. On page 556, the text lists examples of light impurities: urine of animals whose meat is eaten (like horse), dung of birds not eaten (like chicken), and states that less than a quarter of the garment of such light impurities is excused even if it exceeds a dirham size. The author clarifies that donkey urine is specifically mentioned to distinguish it from saliva, and cat and mouse urine are mentioned to refute those who consider them pure. Then 'dung and elephant dung' are defined: rawth for equines, khutha for bovines and elephants, ba'rah for sheep and camels, khar' for birds, najw for dogs, and 'adhirat/gha'it for humans. All animal waste is impure except bird droppings (which are light). Finally, the word 'afw (excused) is explained: it relates to the validity of prayer, not to sin. Actually, performing prayer with an excused amount is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), so washing it is obligatory. Less than the excused amount is makruh tanzihi, so washing is recommended. The reasoning is based on ijma and athar (narrations), and the specific measures are derived from the practice of istinja' with stones, which only dry the area without fully removing impurity. The base of the stool area is about a dirham, so that measure was set for thick impurities. For light impurities, the measure of less than a quarter of the garment was chosen because the quarter often takes the ruling of the whole. This reflects Hanafi juristic reasoning (istihsan) in deriving practical concessions.

Key Terms
نجس غليظ (najas ghaliẓ): A thick impurity that is transmitted from a source considered severely impure, such as human urine, blood, wine, and dung of animals not eaten. Excused up to a dirham.
نجس مخفف (najas khafif): A light impurity, such as urine of animals whose meat is eaten, dung of birds not eaten, etc. Excused up to less than a quarter of the garment.
عفو ('afw): Excused; the amount of impurity that does not invalidate prayer, though leaving it is disliked.
قدر الدرهم (qadr al-dirham): The size of a silver dirham coin, approximately the area of a circle with diameter about 2.5 cm. Used as the measure for excused thick impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the excused amount for thick impurity (najas ghaliẓ) according to the Hanafi school?
A) Less than a quarter of the garment
B) The size of a dirham
C) Any amount is excused if it is from an animal whose meat is eaten
D) There is no excused amount for thick impurities

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits prayer with small amounts of impurity—up to a dirham for thick and less than a quarter of the garment for light—but it is still disliked to leave them unwashed.

Excusable Amounts of Impurities in Prayer
Defines types and quantities of impurities excused in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the excusable amounts for light and heavy impurities, explain the Hanafi ruling regarding keeping such amounts, and distinguish between the three interpretations of 'quarter of the garment'.

Core Idea
Prayer is valid with excused impurities, but keeping them is makruh – thresholds vary by impurity type.

This lesson from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains which impurities are excused in prayer and in what amounts. The Hanafi school distinguishes between light and heavy impurities, setting different thresholds: less than a quarter of the garment for light impurities and less than a dirham for heavy impurities. However, the definition of 'quarter' and 'dirham' involves nuanced intra-madhhab differences. Understanding these thresholds is essential for determining the validity of one's prayer and the obligation to wash impure garments.

Guided Path
1
Identify and classify impurities
List the impurities mentioned in the text and categorize them as light (mukhaffaf) or heavy (mughallaz). Note that the text explicitly mentions chicken, donkey urine, cat/mouse urine, horse dung, cow/beele dung, and less than a quarter of garment of light impurities like horse urine, edible meat droppings, and bird droppings.
Bird droppings are considered pure in Hanafi; other animal droppings are impure.
2
Understand the ruling angle
Recognize that 'afw' (excused) applies to the validity of prayer (sahih), not to sin. Keeping the excused amount is makruh tahrimi (obligatory to wash); less than that is makruh tanzihi (sunnah to wash).
The ruling is from the commentators of Kanz and Munyah.
3
Analyze the threshold for light impurities
Examine the three interpretations of 'quarter of the garment': (1) quarter of the minimum garment for prayer, (2) quarter of the part touched by impurity, (3) Abu Yusuf: one span by one span. Identify which is preferred and why.
Al-Aqta' considers the first as 'asahh ma warada', while al-Muhit and others prefer the second.
4
Analyze the threshold for heavy impurities
Understand that the dirham is measured by weight (mithqal) for thick impurities and by area (palm width) for thin impurities. This reconciles Muhammad's varying narrations.
Thick impurities are those whose substance is visible; thin are those only leaving a trace.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
Determine the relied-upon position: for light impurities, the quarter of the affected part; for heavy impurities, less than a dirham with the appropriate measurement (weight or area). This is the operative fatwa.
The tarjih is based on authentication by later Hanafi authorities.

(Impurities of) chicken, donkey urine, cat, mouse, horse/bedouin dung, goat/sheep droppings, and less than a quarter of the garment from light impurities such as horse urine, and what is edible meat, and droppings of inedible birds are excused. And if it exceeds (that amount) it is not (excused). This is apparent as verified in Radd al-Muhtar. His statement: 'and donkey urine' – he specifically mentioned it to dispel the illusion that its urine is like its saliva (i.e., pure). As for the cat: with kasr of ha and tashdid of ra, Persian: gurba, and mouse Persian: mush, he explicitly mentioned their urine to refute those jurists who consider them pure. His statement: 'roath and khitha' – roath (with fath of ra) is from horse, mule, and donkey, similar to human excrement; khitha (with kasr and sukun) is from cow and elephant; ba'r is from sheep and camel; just as khara is for birds, najw for dog, and 'adhira and gha'it for humans. This is in Radd al-Muhtar. In summary, the excrement of every animal is impure except birds. His statement: 'excused' – i.e., regarding the validity of prayer with it, not regarding sin. Indeed, keeping the excused amount and performing prayer with it is makruh tahrimi, so washing it is obligatory. As for less than that, it is makruh tanzihi, so washing it is sunnah. This is verified by the commentators of Kanz and Munyah. The reasoning is that ijma and athar indicate that a certain amount of impurity is excused and there is no obligation to remove every impurity even if small. So we determined it as less than a quarter for light impurities because the quarter has the ruling of the whole in many rulings, and by the dirham for heavy impurities derived from the ahadith of istinja with stones, for it is known that it is drying and wiping, not cleansing, and the Lawgiver pardoned it, and the place of stool is about the size of a dirham. And the weight of the dirham is considered as the weight of a mithqal in thick (impurity), and its area as the width of the palm in thin. It was said: what is meant by quarter of the garment is quarter of the minimum garment in which prayer is valid. And it was said: quarter of the part that the impurity touched, like the hem, sleeve, and gusset. And Abu Yusuf estimated it as one span by one span. And the weight of the dirham is considered as the weight of a mithqal in thick, and its area as the width of the palm in thin. What is meant by width of palm is the width of the hollow of the palm, which is inside the knuckles. His statement: 'what is meant by quarter of the garment' – al-Aqta said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: this is the most authentic what is reported. His statement: 'and it was said' – its conclusion is that what matters is quarter of the part that the impurity touched of the garment, and quarter of the affected limb like hand and foot, and this statement is authenticated in al-Muhit, al-Tuhfah, al-Mujtaba, and others. His statement: 'and consider...' etc. – since the interpretation of the dirham from Muhammad varies, sometimes he interpreted it as the width of the palm, sometimes as a mithqal which is twenty qirats, they reconciled between them by saying the first interpretation applies when the impurity is heavy and thin, and the second when it is thick, i.e., what the eye observes its substance not only its trace. The second statement: quarter of the entire garment and body, and it is authenticated in al-Mabsut and chosen by the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar. The third statement: quarter of the minimum garment in which prayer is valid, like the lower wrapper; al-Aqta said: this is the most authentic what is reported.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling on excusable amounts of impurities (najasah) in prayer. The passage type is a definition and ruling: it lists specific impurities that are considered 'light' (mukhaffaf) and 'heavy' (mughallaz) and states the maximum amounts that are excused (ma'fu). The author first enumerates excused items: chicken (dajaj), donkey urine (baul himar), cat (hirah), mouse (fa'rah), horse dung (rawth), cow/beele dung (khitha), and anything less than a quarter of a garment from light impurities like horse urine, meat of edible animals, and droppings of birds not eaten. The commentary clarifies that this is binding as the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). He then explains each term: donkey urine was singled out to counter any notion it is pure like saliva; cat and mouse urine are impure, refuting those who claim purity. He defines rawth (horse, mule, donkey dung), khitha (cow, elephant dung), ba'r (sheep, camel droppings), khar' (bird droppings), najw (dog stool), and 'adhira/gha'it (human excrement). Importantly, all animal excrement is impure except that of birds (opinion: birds are also impure? No, the text says 'except birds' meaning bird excrement is pure? Actually the saying 'عذرة كل حيوان نجس غير الطيور' implies bird droppings are not najis? This is a specific Hanafi position: bird droppings are pure. This is noted.) Next, the author explains that 'afw' (excused) means the prayer is valid with such amounts, but it is not without sin: keeping the excused amount is makruh tahrimi (obligatory to wash), and less than that is makruh tanzihi (recommended to wash). The rationale is that ijma and textual evidence show that a small amount is pardoned; the limit for light impurities is less than a quarter of the garment, because a quarter has the status of the whole in many rulings. For heavy impurities the limit is less than a dirham, derived from the practice of istinja with stones, which dry but do not remove all impurity; the area of the stool is about a dirham. The passage then discusses the measurement of the dirham: in thick (kathif) impurities, its weight is the mithqal (approx. 4.25g); in thin (raqiq) impurities, its area is the width of the palm. It presents three opinions on what is meant by 'quarter of the garment': (1) quarter of the minimum garment allowed for prayer (e.g., a loincloth), (2) quarter of the part touched by impurity (e.g., hem, sleeve), (3) from Abu Yusuf: one span by one span. The third is strongest per some authorities. Finally, the reconciliation of Muhammad's different interpretations of the dirham: once as palm width (for thin heavy impurities) and once as mithqal weight (for thick impurities). The second opinion on quarter (entire garment or body) is also considered. This passage is an expansion probe by the commentator, explaining the matn and providing internal Hanafi differences.

Key Terms
عفو (ʿafw): Excused amount of impurity that does not invalidate prayer but is makruh to keep.
ربع الثوب (rubʿ al-thawb): Quarter of the garment; interpreted variably as quarter of minimum prayer garment, or quarter of the part touched.
درهم (dirham): A unit of weight (approx. 3.12g) and area (about palm width) used as threshold for heavy impurities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Hanafi school excuses less than a quarter of the garment for light impurities and less than a dirham for heavy impurities, with specific measurements tied to scholarly interpretation.

The Dirham Measure for Impurity: Weight vs Area
Defines the weight and area measure of a dirham for ruling on impurities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the dirham measure for thick and thin impurities and identify the relied-upon position regarding the quarter of the affected part.

Core Idea
The dirham measure differs by impurity type: weight for thick, area for thin; the excused amount is a quarter of the affected part.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines two distinct measures of the dirham used in Hanafi fiqh for determining when impurity is excused. Understanding this concept is essential for correctly applying the ruling on najasah in prayer. The text also resolves an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the quarter refers to the whole garment or only the affected part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of impurity and their measures
Read the matn: weight (mithqal) for thick impurity, area (width of palm) for thin impurity. Note the definitions of 'thick' (solid, visible) and 'thin' (liquid, staining).
The term 'dirham' here is a legal convention, not necessarily the silver coin weight.
2
Understand the width of the palm
The commentary specifies 'the hollow of the palm' – the inside of the hand at the base of the fingers. This gives a consistent area measure for thin impurities.
Abu Yusuf estimated a span by a span, but the majority used palm width.
3
Distinguish the three opinions on 'quarter of the garment'
Some said quarter of the minimum garment for prayer, others said quarter of the entire garment/body, and the preferred view is quarter of the affected part only (e.g., sleeve, tail).
The relied-upon position is quarter of the affected part, as stated in al-Muhit, al-Tuhfa, etc.
4
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
The dirham measure is applied to the affected part, not the whole garment. For thick impurity, weigh the stain; for thin, compare area to palm width.
This balances strictness and ease, as affirmed by later Hanafi authorities.

(5) Also declared correct by the author of Majma' al-Anhur 1:63, and preferred by the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar 1:214, and followed by the author of Tuhfat al-Muluk p.70. And the second saying: the quarter of the entire garment and body, and declared correct in al-Mabsut 1:55, and chosen by the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar 1:213. And the third saying: the quarter of the minimum garment in which prayer is permissible, like the waist-wrapper, al-Aqta' said: This is the most authentic what has been reported about it. See: Muntaha al-Niqayah 2:98, and Nafahat al-Suluk p.71, and others.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and ruling concerning the measure of impurity that is excused or necessitates washing. The base text (matn) states two measures for the dirham: for thick impurity (one that has substance, like blood), the weight is considered (equal to one mithqal, 20 qirats); for thin impurity (one that stains, like urine), the area is considered (the width of the hollow of the palm). The commentary then discusses different interpretations of the phrase 'quarter of the garment' mentioned earlier in the text. Three opinions are presented: (1) quarter of the minimum garment allowed for prayer; (2) quarter of the affected part of the garment or limb; (3) quarter of the entire garment and body. The commentary notes reconciliations, such as Abu Yusuf's estimation of a span by a span for area. The text also explains that the variation in Muhammad's interpretation (width vs. weight) is reconciled by applying the width measure to thick impurities that are thin (i.e., liquid) and the weight measure to thick impurities that are solid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) per later Hanafi authorities is the first interpretation (quarter of the affected part).

Key Terms
الدرهم (al-dirham): A measure of weight (mithqal) for thick impurity and area (width of palm) for thin impurity.
الكثيف (al-kathif): Thick impurity that has visible substance, e.g., blood.
الرقيق (al-raqiq): Thin impurity that spreads and stains, e.g., urine.
عرض الكف (ard al-kaff): The width of the hollow of the palm, approximately the area of a dirham.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is the dirham measure applied for a thick impurity like blood?
A) By weight, equal to one mithqal
B) By area, equal to the width of the palm
C) By volume, equal to a dirham coin
D) It is not excused at all

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the excused amount of impurity is determined by weight (mithqal) for thick and area (palm width) for thin impurities, and the relied-upon position is that only a quarter of the affected part needs to be considered.

Fish Blood and Saliva of Mule/Donkey
States rulings on fish blood and saliva of mule/donkey with explanatory commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the purity of fish blood and the saliva of mules and donkeys, and explain the underlying principles (nature of blood and doubt).

Core Idea
Fish blood is pure because it is not blood; doubt does not remove established purity.

This lesson covers two purity rulings from the Hanafi school: fish blood and the saliva of mules and donkeys. The commentary explains why fish blood is not considered impure—it is a watery fluid, not real blood—and why the saliva of mules and donkeys does not nullify purity, based on the principle that doubt cannot overcome certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substances and their rulings
Fish blood is ruled not impure (ghayr najis). Saliva of mules and donkeys does not make a pure thing impure (la yunajjis tahiran). Note the ruling: both are considered pure or at least not removing purity.
2
Understand the basis for fish blood
The commentary states fish blood is not real blood but a watery moisture. Evidence: real blood turns black in sunlight, fish blood turns white. Therefore it is not within the general prohibition of blood.
This is a case of distinguishing by physical properties (dalalah al-shay' bi-khalafihi).
3
Understand the basis for saliva
The saliva of mules and donkeys is considered doubtful in terms of impurity (mashkuk). The garment was originally pure (yaqin), and doubt (shakk) does not remove certainty. Hence prayer is permissible.
The principle: al-yaqin la yuzalu bi al-shakk.

Source Text

[0] قوله : لا رماد ؛ أي رماد القذر ليس بنجس، وكذا ملح كان حماراً أو غيره من الحيوانات، فوقع في المملحة ، فانقلب ملحا طاهر، بشرط أن لا يكون نجس العين، وذلك لأن انقلاب العين يزيل وصف النجاسة، فإنّ زوال الذات مستلزم لزوال الوصف.
(۱) المراد به العذرة والروث. ينظر : (رد المحتار» (۱ : ۲۱۷).

Fish blood is not impure, and the saliva of the mule and donkey does not make a pure thing impure, and urine that splashes like the heads of needles is nothing, and water that comes upon impurity is impure like its opposite, no ash from dung, and salt that was [from] a donkey, and fish blood is not impure, and the saliva of the mule and donkey does not make a pure thing impure) because it is doubtful, so the pure does not lose its purity by doubt. [0] [1] (3) (And urine that splashes like the heads of needles is nothing, and water that comes upon impurity, impure like its opposite): i.e., just as water is impure in its opposite, which is the coming of impurity upon water. (No ash from dung, and salt that was [from] a donkey): i.e., none of them is impure. And in [1] His saying: it is not impure because it is not real blood, but rather a watery moisture similar to it, evidenced by the fact that blood when the sun hits it turns black, while fish blood turns white. [2] His saying: because it is doubtful; meaning that if the saliva of a mule or donkey touches the garment or body, prayer is permissible with it because it is doubtful, as previously verified in the discussion of remnants, and doubt does not remove certainty, so the purity of the garment established with certainty is not removed by it. (3) [3] His saying: like the heads of needles; with a kasra on the hamza and fatha on the ba, plural of ibrah which is the needle in which thread is threaded and with which cloth is sewn, called in Persian 'sozan', and the restriction to the head indicates that if it were to the extent of the other side, washing becomes necessary, and some scholars said: the sides are not considered to avoid hardship. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. [4] His saying: and water that comes; subject, its predicate is 'impure' the second, with a kasra on the jim, and the first with a fatha, and it is permissible to put kasra on both, meaning whatever runs over something impure or impurity becomes impure, just as water upon which impurity comes is impure due to the existence of mixing with impurity. [0] His saying: no ash; i.e., ash from filth is not impure, and likewise salt that was from a donkey or other animals, and it fell into the salt store and turned into salt is pure, on condition that it is not inherently impure, because transformation of the substance removes the description of impurity, as the removal of the essence necessitates removal of the description. (1) What is meant by it is feces and dung. See: Radd al-Mukhtar 1:217. (2) pp. 209-210. (3) Fath al-Qadir 1:209, and see: Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 1:75, and Majma' al-Anhur 1:63.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings concerning purity and impurity. The base text lists: (1) Fish blood is not impure — the commentary explains it is not real blood but a watery fluid; real blood turns black in the sun, fish blood turns white. (2) Saliva of mules and donkeys does not nullify the purity of a pure object — this is due to doubt (shakk) about its impurity; certainty of purity is not overcome by doubt. Therefore, if such saliva touches a garment, prayer is still valid. (3) Urine that splashes in amounts like the heads of needles is excused and does not require washing — the commentary specifies that the head of the needle is the tip; if the amount equals the other side (the eye), it would require washing, but some scholars disregard the sides to avoid hardship. (4) Water that falls upon an impurity becomes impure, just as the reverse (impurity falling into water) makes it impure — due to mixing. (5) Ash from dung (filth) is not impure — because the essence has changed by burning. Similarly, salt that was from a donkey (i.e., its body or dung) that falls into a salt bed and turns into salt becomes pure, provided the original substance was not inherently impure (najis al-ayn), because transformation (istihala) removes the property of impurity.

Key Terms
دم السمك (dam al-samak): Fish blood, which according to the Hanafi school is not impure because it is not considered blood in the legal sense.
لعاب (lu'ab): Saliva of a mule or donkey, which is considered doubtful in terms of impurity; the original purity is maintained.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, fish blood is considered:
A) Impure
B) Pure
C) Doubtful
D) Not applicable

Key Takeaway
Fish blood is pure; saliva of mule/donkey does not nullify purity due to the principle that doubt does not remove certainty.

Tiny Urine Spots and Water Encountering Impurity
States rulings on urine spots and water encountering impurity with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on negligible amounts of impurity (urine spots) and on water that falls upon an impurity, and explain the criteria for each.

Core Idea
Tiny amounts of impurity are excused; water that mixes with impurity becomes impure.

This lesson addresses two related purity issues: How much urine on a garment requires washing? And what happens when water contacts an impurity? The Hanafi school provides clear guidance: negligible amounts like the head of a needle are excused, while water that falls on impurity becomes impure due to mixing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Case 1: Urine that splashes in an amount like the heads of needles (small dots). Case 2: Water that comes upon an impurity (e.g., pours over something najis).
2
Ruling for urine spots
Such a tiny amount is 'nothing' (laysa bi shay') – it is excused and does not require washing. The commentary specifies that only the tip of the needle is considered; if the amount equals the other side (the eye), it must be washed. Some scholars disregard both sides to avoid hardship.
This is a ruling based on tolerance (hardship principle: daf' al-haraj).
3
Ruling for water on impurity
Water that falls upon an impurity (or impurity falls upon water) becomes impure (najis) due to mixing. The impurity permeates the water. This is symmetrical: both 'water on impurity' and 'impurity in water' yield impure water.
The key is that mixing occurs; the water loses its purity.

Transformation of Impurities: Ash from Dung and Salt from Donkey
States rulings on ash from dung and salt from donkey with commentary on transformation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of istihala (transformation) and apply it to determine the purity of ash from dung and salt from an animal body.

Core Idea
Transformation (istihala) removes impurity; ash from dung and salt from a donkey are pure because their essence changed.

This lesson examines two cases where a substance that was originally impure becomes pure through a change in its essence. The Hanafi school applies the principle of istihala: when the substance is transformed into something entirely different, the impurity is removed. The commentary adds the important condition that the original substance must not be inherently impure (najis al-ayn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two substances and their origins
Case 1: Ash (ramad) from dung (qadhr, meaning feces or dry dung). Case 2: Salt (milh) that was originally from a donkey (i.e., its body or dung turned into salt).
2
State the ruling
Both are pure (tahir) and not impure. The ash is not impure, and the salt is not impure.
3
Understand the principle of istihala
The commentary explains that transformation (inqilab) of the substance removes the description of impurity. When the essence (al-'ayn) changes, the ruling of impurity is lifted because the impurity no longer exists.
Key condition: the original must not be najis al-ayn (inherently impure, e.g., pig, dog). Dung and donkey are not najis al-ayn in the Hanafi school.
4
Examine the condition
The commentary states 'bi-sharti an la yakuna najis al-'ayn' – on condition that it is not inherently impure. This ensures that only impure substances that can become pure by transformation are included.
This distinction prevents impure animals like pigs from being purified by salt transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودمُ السمك ليس بنجس، ولعاب البغل، والحمار لا ينجس طاهراً، وبول انتضحَ مثل رؤوس الإبر ليس بشيء، وماءٌ وَرَدَ على نجس ، نجس كعكسه لا رماد قدر، وملح كان حماراً ودم السمك ليس بنجس، ولعاب البغل، والحمار لا ينجس طاهراً) ؛ لأنه مشكوك " ، فالطاهر لا تزول طهارته بالشَّكَ.
[0]
[1]
(۳)
(وبول انتضحَ مثل رؤوس الإبر ليس بشيء، وماءٌ وَرَدَ على نَجس، نجس كعكسه ) : أى كما أن الماءَ نَجَسٌ في عكسه، وهو ورود النجاسة على الماء. (لا رماد قدر ) ، وملح كان حماراً) : أي لا يكون شيء منهما نجساً ، وفي [١]قوله : ليس بنجس؛ لأنه ليس بدم حقيقة، بل رطوبة مائية شبيهة به، بدليل أن الدم إذا أصابته الشمس اسود ودم السمك يبيض. [٢]قوله : لأنه مشكوك ؛ يعني إذا أصاب الثوبَ والبدن لعاب البغل والحمار تجوزُ الصلاة به؛ لأنه مشكواً على ما مرَّ تحقيقه في «بحث السؤر ) ، والشك لا يزيلُ اليقين، فلا تزولُ به طهارة الثوب الثابتة باليقين.
(۳)
[٣] قوله : مثل رؤوس الإبر ؛ بكسر الهمزة وفتح الباء الموحدة، جمع الإبرة وهي : المخيط الذي ينظم فيه الخيط، ويخاط به الثوب ، يقال له بالفارسية : سوزن ، والتقييد بالرأس يفيد أنه لو أصابه بقدر الجانب الآخر يلزمُ غسله، ومن المشايخ من قال : لا يعتبر الجانبان ؛ دفعاً للحرج. كذا في «فتح القدير» [٤] قوله : وماء ورد ؛ مبتدأ خبره نجس الثاني وهو بكسر الجيم ، والأول بفتحها، ويجوز الكسر فيهما ، يعني ما جرى على شيءٍ نجس أو نجاسة نجس ، كما أنّ الماء الوارد عليه النجس نجس ؛ لوجود الاختلاط بالنجاسة. [0] قوله : لا رماد ؛ أي رماد القذر ليس بنجس، وكذا ملح كان حماراً أو غيره من الحيوانات، فوقع في المملحة ، فانقلب ملحا طاهر، بشرط أن لا يكون نجس العين، وذلك لأن انقلاب العين يزيل وصف النجاسة، فإنّ زوال الذات مستلزم لزوال الوصف.
(۱) المراد به العذرة والروث. ينظر : (رد المحتار» (۱ : ۲۱۷).
(۲) (ص۲۰۹ - ۲۱۰).
(۳) «فتح القدير (۱) : (۲۰۹) ، وينظر : تبيين الحقائق (۱) : (۷۵) ، و (مجمع الأنهر» (١: ٦٣).
Fish blood is not impure, and the saliva of the mule and donkey does not make a pure thing impure, and urine that splashes like the heads of needles is nothing, and water that comes upon impurity is impure like its opposite, no ash from dung, and salt that was [from] a donkey, and fish blood is not impure, and the saliva of the mule and donkey does not make a pure thing impure) because it is doubtful, so the pure does not lose its purity by doubt. [0] [1] (3) (And urine that splashes like the heads of needles is nothing, and water that comes upon impurity, impure like its opposite): i.e., just as water is impure in its opposite, which is the coming of impurity upon water. (No ash from dung, and salt that was [from] a donkey): i.e., none of them is impure. And in [1] His saying: it is not impure because it is not real blood, but rather a watery moisture similar to it, evidenced by the fact that blood when the sun hits it turns black, while fish blood turns white. [2] His saying: because it is doubtful; meaning that if the saliva of a mule or donkey touches the garment or body, prayer is permissible with it because it is doubtful, as previously verified in the discussion of remnants, and doubt does not remove certainty, so the purity of the garment established with certainty is not removed by it. (3) [3] His saying: like the heads of needles; with a kasra on the hamza and fatha on the ba, plural of ibrah which is the needle in which thread is threaded and with which cloth is sewn, called in Persian 'sozan', and the restriction to the head indicates that if it were to the extent of the other side, washing becomes necessary, and some scholars said: the sides are not considered to avoid hardship. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. [4] His saying: and water that comes; subject, its predicate is 'impure' the second, with a kasra on the jim, and the first with a fatha, and it is permissible to put kasra on both, meaning whatever runs over something impure or impurity becomes impure, just as water upon which impurity comes is impure due to the existence of mixing with impurity. [0] His saying: no ash; i.e., ash from filth is not impure, and likewise salt that was from a donkey or other animals, and it fell into the salt store and turned into salt is pure, on condition that it is not inherently impure, because transformation of the substance removes the description of impurity, as the removal of the essence necessitates removal of the description. (1) What is meant by it is feces and dung. See: Radd al-Mukhtar 1:217. (2) pp. 209-210. (3) Fath al-Qadir 1:209, and see: Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 1:75, and Majma' al-Anhur 1:63.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings concerning purity and impurity. The base text lists: (1) Fish blood is not impure — the commentary explains it is not real blood but a watery fluid; real blood turns black in the sun, fish blood turns white. (2) Saliva of mules and donkeys does not nullify the purity of a pure object — this is due to doubt (shakk) about its impurity; certainty of purity is not overcome by doubt. Therefore, if such saliva touches a garment, prayer is still valid. (3) Urine that splashes in amounts like the heads of needles is excused and does not require washing — the commentary specifies that the head of the needle is the tip; if the amount equals the other side (the eye), it would require washing, but some scholars disregard the sides to avoid hardship. (4) Water that falls upon an impurity becomes impure, just as the reverse (impurity falling into water) makes it impure — due to mixing. (5) Ash from dung (filth) is not impure — because the essence has changed by burning. Similarly, salt that was from a donkey (i.e., its body or dung) that falls into a salt bed and turns into salt becomes pure, provided the original substance was not inherently impure (najis al-ayn), because transformation (istihala) removes the property of impurity.

Key Terms
استحالة (istihala): Transformation of the essence of a substance; a legal principle that removes impurity when the original substance is not inherently impure.
رماد قدر (ramad qadir): Ash from dung (filth); it is pure because the essence changes by burning.
ملح كان حماراً (milh kana himaran): Salt that was originally from a donkey (e.g., its body or dung) but transformed into salt; it becomes pure if the original was not inherently impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, ash from dried cow dung is considered:
A) Impure, because dung is impure
B) Pure, due to transformation (istihala)
C) Pure only if washed
D) Excused but still ritually impure

Key Takeaway
Ash from dung and salt from a donkey are pure due to istihala, as long as the original is not inherently impure (najis al-ayn).

Fish Blood: A Pure Substance by Nature
Ruling with empirical proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why fish blood is ruled pure in the Hanafi school and identify the empirical proof used.

Core Idea
Fish blood is not impure because it is not true blood but a watery moisture.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling that fish blood is pure. The text provides a simple but powerful empirical proof: true blood blackens under the sun, while fish blood whitens. This shows that fish blood is a different substance altogether, and thus the general impurity of blood does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the text: 'Fish blood is not impure.' The hukm level is that it is pure (tahir).
2
Locate the proof
The proof is given in the commentary: fish blood is not real blood but a watery moisture, as evidenced by its reaction to sunlight.
3
Analyze the evidence
The empirical observation (true blood blackens, fish blood whitens) serves as the dalil. This is a form of qiyas: comparing fish blood to true blood shows they are different, so the ruling differs.
This proof is not from Quran or hadith but from observable reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(لا رماد قدر ) ، وملح كان حماراً) : أي لا يكون شيء منهما نجساً ، وفي [١]قوله : ليس بنجس؛ لأنه ليس بدم حقيقة، بل رطوبة مائية شبيهة به، بدليل أن الدم إذا أصابته الشمس اسود ودم السمك يبيض. [0] قوله : لا رماد ؛ أي رماد القذر ليس بنجس، وكذا ملح كان حماراً أو غيره من الحيوانات، فوقع في المملحة ، فانقلب ملحا طاهر، بشرط أن لا يكون نجس العين، وذلك لأن انقلاب العين يزيل وصف النجاسة، فإنّ زوال الذات مستلزم لزوال الوصف. (۱) المراد به العذرة والروث. ينظر : (رد المحتار» (۱ : ۲۱۷). (۲) (ص۲۰۹ - ۲۱۰). (۳) «فتح القدير (۱) : (۲۰۹) ، وينظر : تبيين الحقائق (۱) : (۷۵) ، و (مجمع الأنهر» (١: ٦٣). وينظر: «التنبيه»(۱ : ۱۷)، و(المنهاج وشرحه (مغني المحتاج (۱): ۸۱)، فإن عبارتها تدل على خلاف الشافعي في مسألة رماد القذر، ومسألة ملح كان حماراً ؛ لأنه لا يطهر نجس العين عندهم إلا خمر تخللت خمراً وجلداً نجس بالموت فيطهر بدبغه.
Fish blood is not impure, and the saliva of the mule and donkey does not make a pure thing impure. And urine that splashes like the head of a needle is nothing. And water that comes upon impurity becomes impure, like its opposite. Not ash of a stove, and salt that was from a donkey. And fish blood is not impure, and the saliva of the mule and donkey does not make a pure thing impure; because it is doubtful, and the pure does not lose its purity by doubt. (And urine that splashes like the head of a needle is nothing, and water that comes upon impurity becomes impure like its opposite): meaning just as the water is impure in its opposite, which is the coming of impurity upon water. (Not ash of a stove, and salt that was from a donkey): meaning none of them is impure. And in [1] his saying: not impure; because it is not real blood, but a watery moisture resembling it, as evidence that when blood is exposed to the sun it blackens, while fish blood whitens. [2] His saying: because it is doubtful; meaning if the saliva of the mule or donkey touches the garment or body, it is permissible to pray with it; because it is doubtful as previously detailed in the 'discussion of remnants', and doubt does not remove certainty, so the purity of the garment established with certainty is not removed by it. [3] His saying: like the heads of needles; with kasra of the hamza and fatha of the ba, plural of needle, which is the sewing tool through which thread is passed and with which cloth is sewn, called in Persian 'suzan'. The qualification with the head indicates that if it touches to the extent of the other side, washing becomes obligatory. And some mashayikh said: both sides are not considered, to avoid hardship. Meaning what flows over something impure or impurity becomes impure, just as water upon which impurity falls is impure due to the existence of mixing with impurity. [0] His saying: not ash; meaning the ash of filth is not impure, and likewise salt that was from a donkey or other animals, that falls into the salt cellar and turns into salt is pure, provided that it is not an impure substance itself, because the transformation of substance removes the description of impurity, for the removal of the essence necessitates the removal of the description. (1) By it is meant filth and dung. See: Radd al-Muhtar 1:217. (2) pp. 209-210. (3) Fath al-Qadir 1:209, and see: Tabyin al-Haqaiq 1:75, and Majma' al-Anhur 1:63. And see: al-Tanbih 1:17, and al-Minhaj and its explanation (Mughni al-Muhtaj 1:81), for their expressions indicate the difference of al-Shafi'i regarding the issue of ash of filth and the issue of salt that was from a donkey; because an impure substance is not purified in their school except wine that turns into vinegar, and skin that is najis due to death that is purified by tanning.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This commentary explains several rulings on impurities. Fish blood is not impure because it is not real blood; proof is that it whitens in the sun whereas true blood blackens. Saliva of mules and donkeys is doubtful, so it does not remove the certainty of purity. A tiny amount of urine (size of a needle head) is excused due to hardship. Water becomes impure when it contacts impurity, whether the impurity is solid or liquid. However, ash from burnt filth and salt that has transformed from animal origin are pure because the essence changed. The Hanafi position on transformation differs from the Shafi'i school, which only allows purification through transformation for wine into vinegar and tanned skin.

Key Terms
دم السمك (dam al-samak): Fish blood; ruled pure because it is not true blood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is fish blood considered pure in the Hanafi school?
A) Because it is from a halal animal
B) Because it is not real blood
C) Because it is doubtful
D) Because it is very little

Key Takeaway
Fish blood is pure because it is not real blood; the ruling is based on the substance's true nature.

Mule and Donkey Saliva: Doubt Does Not Remove Certainty
Ruling based on the principle of doubt vs certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that doubt does not remove certainty to the case of mule and donkey saliva.

Core Idea
Saliva of mules and donkeys is excused because certainty of purity is not overridden by doubt.

This lesson covers the ruling that the saliva of mules and donkeys does not render a pure thing impure. The reasoning is based on doubt: since there is conflicting evidence about the impurity of their saliva, the original state of purity remains intact.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
The text says: 'the saliva of the mule and donkey does not make a pure thing impure.'
2
Identify the principle
The reason given is 'because it is doubtful.' The principle is that doubt does not remove certainty (al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk).
3
Apply the principle
Since the purity of the garment is established with certainty, a doubtful impurity does not negate it.

Source Text
